O children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, it seems to me that by its completion and explanation, I fulfill my promise to you with this treatise on surgery.
Because the practice of surgery is almost unknown in our region and time, it is feared that this science will be lost, its traces will disappear, and only a few small descriptions will remain in the books of the Ancients. But these are obscure and their meaning is difficult to understand.
And it seems to me that I should revive this with a treatise arranged in such a way as to explain, clarify and abbreviate; and also include illustrations of the forms of cautery tools and other instruments used in the operation, both for addition to the explanation and preparation of the subject matter.
But the reason why skilled surgeons are not found in our time is that the art of medicine is lengthy and requires its practitioner to be well versed in the science of anatomy before attempting surgery. One must know the arrangement of the limbs, the forms of their joints and separations; the number and function of bones, nerves, and ligaments; and the location of arterial and venous pulsation points.
"Thus says Hippocrates that in name it is small, but in work it is great, especially in the art of the hand."
"But we have already spoken of this at the beginning of this book; for whoever does not know what we have said about anatomy will not avoid falling into error which often causes the death of men, as I have seen too many times, since some are formed in this science but lack knowledge and experience."
"I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician who incised an aphemphous abscess on the neck of a woman, cutting some arteries in the process, causing so much blood to flow that the woman suddenly died between her sheets."
"And I saw another physician who, shortly after removing a stone from a man in his urinary state, and the stone was large, he tore part of the bladder with it, causing that man to die on the third day."
"And I had already warned him not to do so, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the state of the illness, I knew right away that he would die."
"And I saw another physician who received payment of one ducat from our land for surgery, and he came to treat a black man who had a broken thighbone. And through his ignorance, the physician put splints on it with bandages and braces too tightly, not allowing space for respiration."
"And he released him according to his desires, and then left him, leaving him like that for some days and ordering that the bandages not be removed. And thus it was until the thighbone and flesh became putrid, resulting in harm."
"And I was called to him, and I prepared to remove the bandages, and thus tranquility returned and he recovered from his pain."
"But however, corruption had already become established in the member, so that I could not prevent it, and corruption did not cease to wander through the member until the patient died."
"And I saw another physician who punctured a cancerous boil, and it became ulcerated some days later, during which time the evil of it increased with the patient."
"The reason is that black bile is pure of humor, so it should not be cauterized with fire at all, but rather it should be treated with members which suffer from heat."
"And for that reason, my children, it is necessary that the operation be divided into two parts: one is the operation which is associated with health, and one is the work in which there is danger in the greatest dispositions."
"And I have already spoken about this in all parts of this book where operation is mentioned which contains error and fear. Therefore, it is necessary for you to observe this and leave that, so that the foolish may not find an opportunity to speak and dishonor you."
"And therefore take your weapons with solicitude and protection from God, and your illnesses with patience and firmness."
"And use the best way through the grace of God to achieve salvation and blessed success, and leave behind terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and bear your weapons against that which causes you fear, so that you may not waver in your faith and in your name: for more remains to your glory, both here and hereafter, and your sanctity."
"Because it has already been said in some of my books:" "'Do not treat an illness that you do not know, lest you be called a bad physician.'"
"And I have already divided this book according to three chapters."
"Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part." "The first chapter deals with the head in its forms of instruments and cautery irons, and what is necessary for the operation."
"The division of the second part." "The second chapter, on stitching and puncturing, leeching, cupping, extracting arrows and similar things, all organized according to the forms of instruments."
"The third chapter, on reduction and dislocation, and the cure of torsion and similar things; this chapter deals with the head in its weights and forms of instruments."
"It is necessary for me to say what are the virtues of that cautery and its harms, and in which complexion cautery is prohibited."
"Therefore I say that the discourse on the virtue of cautery and its harm is long and prolix, subtle in science, and secretly hidden; many wise men have already spoken about it, and they disagree among themselves, and it suffices me to mention a little of their discourses for fear of lengthiness."
"And therefore I say that cautery confers benefit to all complexions universally, whether they have matter or not, except for two complexions which are without matter."
"In the complexion that is cold and dry with matter there will be disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable for an illness that is caused by quality and dryness, because nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is not fitting that an illness that is cold and dry with matter should be healed with something hot and dry."
"And he who says the contrary of this, that cautery brings benefit to all cold and dry illnesses that occur in men's bodies, for when you compare the body of man and its humidity with the complexion of fire, you find the body of man inferior."
"And I say according to what he says, that experience has already proven this to me on several occasions."
"But nevertheless it is not necessary to elaborate on this subject that has already been discussed and exercised in the chapter on cautery at length, and after having studied the diversity of complexion in men and the disposition of illnesses in their members, their causes, accidents, and the space of time for each."
"But in other complexions there is no cause for fear, especially in hot and humid illnesses, because all physicians agree on these and are not in disagreement about the benefit of cautery in them."
"And know, my son, that the secret of curing by fire cautery and its goodness consists in cautery with combustible medicine, because fire is simple and its action only reaches to the member being cauterized, and does not reach another member nearby except with slight harm."
"But the action of combustible medicine reaches to that which it touches, and strong heat can bring about an illness in a nearby member that is difficult to heal, and strong heat also exits."
"But fire, because of its sublimity and goodness of substance, does not do anything except superfluously. And this is already known to us through experience and long study of the art with regard to the truth of causes; and for this reason I am excused from lengthening the discussion. And if it were not that my book has not yet been read in this place, you would find hidden here a secret about fire and the quality of its operation in bodies and its expulsion by which illnesses are expelled, demonstrated with philosophical speech, which would sharpen your understanding."
"And know, my son, that they disagree about the time when it is appropriate to perform cautery and they place the best time in spring."
"I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental time is submerged in the judgment of that which is drawn with the cautery, and even more if the cautery is for pains or necessary pains, strong and fixed, which do not tolerate delay because it is feared from those that come malice, which is greater than small entering harm."
"And do not count on yourselves, O children, for that is not esteemed by the common people and foolish doctors, since it is believed that any illness that is healed by cautery is a lasting remedy, and they pause at that consequence."
"And the cause is not as they believe because cautery is only a substitute for medicine which alters the complexion and dries out the humors, which are the cause of pains."
"But however, cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the will of its virtue and operation and the greatness of its Lord."
"And it is possible that his illness will return at some time in the future, depending on the complexion of the sick person and the location of their illness, and its virtue and what is prepared in their body from the accumulation of superfluities therein, and what they have acquired through food and similar causes; but if an illness for which cautery has been administered is a subtle one in a member with few superfluities and humors, like cautery of teeth for pain or similar things, it is possible that this pain will not return, but it is done little."
"And more commonly it is said that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this saying is true, for they believe that there is no cure that profits either with medicine or with any other cause besides cautery."
"And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when other remedies have been tried in certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and therefore cautery is administered at the end, and it profits for that." "And here it is said that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention understood by the common people and many foolish doctors."
"And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance."
"And they said that the place of cautery does not make a scar, which is not absolutely true, for I have seen and proven that this is only so in certain bodies without others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; but nevertheless when you heat cautery with fire with gold it is not declared to you, because it ignites according to the quantity you want due to the roughness of gold, and prepares to receive a burn; and if you adjust the ignition it melts the gold by the fire, and some sheets fall into the fire so that the artist catches them in his occupation."
"And for this reason cautery with iron is done more lightly and more gently with regard to the correctness of the operation."
"And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and those of the head within the weights, so that later when asking for something from it you may easily find what you want."
The first chapter
This cautery confers upon the skin humidity and coldness; its accidental cause is due to two things: namely, heat and excessive flow of phlegm, called catarrh, which flows from the head down to the eyes and ears, causing excessive sleepiness, toothache and throat pain, and generally speaking, all diseases that arise from coldness, such as paralysis, apoplexy, etc., and similar ones.
Firstly, you should command the patient to purge with a laxative medicine for three or four nights, depending on what you judge appropriate according to the severity of their illness and their age and constitution.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and have him sit on a large bench covered with a sheet, with his face turned upwards.
Then place the cautery of your palm just above the tip of their nose between their eyebrows. But first, when you have arrived at that point with your finger, mark it with a pen and then light the olive-oil cautery which is in this shape:
Then place it on the marked spot so that it descends gently, pressing down little by little until you feel the heat. Then twist it slowly back and forth.
Then lift your hand gently and observe the spot; if you see that some of the bone is already exposed, like a grain of barley, then lift your hand. If not, return your hand with the iron, using another one if it has cooled down, until you see what I have told you to look for.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot, leaving it there for three days.
Then place around the edges of the wound and leave it on until the burn scar has healed completely. "And then treat the spot with a fourfold remedy so that it may be healed."
"And they said that whenever the plague breaks out, it is better to tie one's arms tightly, for it brings greater relief and healing."
"And some of them said that the spine should be cauterized within the bone, and that the cautery should be held there until some part of the spinal marrow is adhered."
"Then scrape off what has adhered to the bone, and then treat it."
"And others said that when the cautery reaches the end, it should press strongly onto the bone until the bone cracks, and this is the form of a small alfecati." "And they believe that from that spot the vapors of the head expire."
"And let the plague remain untreated for a long time, and then treat it so that it may be consolidated."
"But I have not seen these two kinds of cauteries performed entirely, but only in some men due to fear; and that one, it seems to me, is better with health when present, for the head is dilated by solution of continuity from this same cause as we have testified regarding other members, and even more so when the head of the sick person is of a hot nature."
"And the first kind of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and with that instrument and that procedure one has health."
"Chapter Two."
"On cauterizing the head."
"When there is chronic and long-lasting pain in the whole head and this persists for a prolonged period of illness, and various remedies are used such as bandages, poultices, compresses, plaster casts, ointments, decoctions, poultices made from plants or animal parts, and similar things, especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that does not help much due to those causes, then consider whether the head of the sick person is of strong constitution in its nature; and if it is not hotter than usual, then cauterize it with another cautery at another place near that one." "And similarly cauterize it on the horn of his head with a cautery until the bone is broken." "Cut the bone and expose it to the quantity we have mentioned, and cauterize there with a cautery at the last part of the head where it is called 'vagus', which is the place where cupping is performed." "And be gentle in this matter and do not expose the bone, for the illness suffers great pain from this different from the pain of all other cauteries on the head."
"And I say this cautery in its place, for it is necessary to cauterize with the cautery that one uses to cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput, which is a more subtle cautery than that used to cauterize the middle part of the head; and this is its form."
"Chapter Three."
"On cauterizing chronic migraine."
"When there is sudden pain in the part of the head or a stabbing pain within it, evacuate with medications that cleanse the head and administer the other treatment which I have mentioned in the remedies for illnesses; and if nothing helps, then cauterize in two ways: either with a caustic medication that burns, or with iron."
"The better cautery with burning medication is this: take some aloe and scrape it and cut off both its heads." "And then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a sharp lancet until you reach the bone beneath it."
"And then press it with a tight bandage for several hours, and leave it alone for three hours; then remove the bandage and remove the aloe, leaving the wound open for two or three days; then bathe the surrounding area with warm water until the location becomes moist."
"And then treat it with ointment until it is healed."
"If you wish, do this with some caustic medications which I have written about in the second book of caustic medicines."
"The better cautery with iron is done in this way: heat the cautery which has this shape:"
"and it is called 'closed nails', for its head is like the shape of a nail with some curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small prominence."
"And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand steady so that the heat does not adhere to the cautery which is underneath; and slowly rotate the hot iron with speed."
"And if the quantity of it that adheres to the bone is thus much, lift your hand up so that the artery below does not adhere, lest blood flow."
"And then immerse the area in salt water and place it there, leaving it alone for three days; then bathe the area with warm water, and treat it with ointment until it is healed."
"And if you wish, cauterize with the point of a knife according to cautery, and beware of incising an artery in this chronic migraine properly."
"Chapter Four."
"On cauterizing acute migraine."
"When you treat migraine with what we have said about the previous treatment and what we have mentioned in the divisions of illnesses, and if the treatment does not help, and the illness is such that it does not reach what we have said about the first cautery with medication or with fire, then heat the knife cautery until it is red hot, and place your hand on the location of the pain with a line of its length, and press it there until you incise the artery and enter to the bone."
"However, it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the mandible, which is moved by chewing, and that the muscle is not adhered to and the nerves that move it, and that spasms do not occur."
"And if it is about the caution and care of blood flow from the artery that is opened, for there is fear in its opening, especially for him who does not know what he is doing and is not studious and experienced; leave the operation at the beginning."
"And I will bring to mind the regimen of accidental bleeding from an artery according to its manner, in its place in this book."
"And if you see that this illness does not benefit from this cautery and that the patient can tolerate it, cauterize him in the middle of his head as we have told, and treat the wound until it is healed."
"And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on tying an artery with cautery having two points, for this is better cautery and more effective."
"Chapter Five."
"On cauterizing earaches."
"When there is pain in the ear due to friction and it is treated with laxative causes and other treatments that have been mentioned in the division, but the pain is not removed, then heat the cautery, which is called 'punctum' and has this shape:"
"And then prick it at the ignition point and lead it around the entire ear like a wheel is turned, or around each prominence if the pain is in each one." "Be careful with the cautery of the ear's opening little by little because you have felt the location with its tip."
"And if you make ten points in all the ear and quit it, then treat those locations until they are healed."
"Chapter Six."
"On cauterizing tortures."
"The torture that is treated with cautery is only of those species that come from phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of diseases."
"Evade cauterization of the species that occurs through dryness or spasm of the nerve."
"When you treat that species of tortura with herbs and capipurgi and gargarisms, and yet you cannot confirm your treatment, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, the second a little behind the cheekbone, and the third at the junction of two lips."
"And place your cautery on the opposite side from where the ailment began, for softening is seen only in the part that seems healed."
"And the manner of cauterization is that you should cauterize it with the cautery on the right side of the extreme tip of the ear, a little above the horn of the head, and another on the cheekbone, its length being equal to the length of the thumb, and then place your hand with the cautery until most of the surface area of the wound is burned."
"And this is the shape of the cautery, and it is a specialty of the barber-surgeon, the shape of which is shown on this table." "Although perhaps it may seem too subtle to you, yet in place of unnecessary roughness it is better to cut a little."
"And then cure the wound with what is denoted as processed before until it is healed."
"Chapter VII."
"On the cautery of ancient apoplexy."
"When apoplexy is ancient and you cure it with what we have said and your medication does not work and the ailment has no fever, then cauterize those places with four cauteries: on all the horns of its head; and a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and a cautery behind the ear." "And the manner of the cautery is made according to what has been said above."
"After it has been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach, and if it is worse, then it should be cured with what you process."
"Chapter VIII."
Burns caused by forgetfulness are treated with flame cautery.
"Great quantities of pills are given to the patient at the beginning, serving to cleanse."
"And then immediately cover the entire head of the patient and place on it the plaster described in the treatise on plasters."
"And apply this several times, for this is a manner of cautery." "And do this according to that method already mentioned."
"If this does not heal well, then make three cauteries in the last section of their head, which should be descending from the most elevated part of the head towards the lowest part of the neck." "And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and immediately cover the cautery with that which has been produced."
"But if the patient can tolerate it, then make a cautery in the middle; and if they want more, then cauterize those over both ears."
"Then cover that which has been healed; and let the cauteries be oval according to the form given above."
Chapter IX
Of cauterization of paralysis and softening of the whole body.
"First, you shall proceed with the preparation of the head using herbs and the things we have mentioned. Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize him in the middle of the head with a cautery. Also cauterize all around the horns of his head, and cauterize at the last part of it. And apply three cauteries on the spinal bones of the neck."
"If the illness of softening of the body is great and the patient cannot tolerate it, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the spinal bones of the back, and afterward cauterize within the hollow of his throat, and lift up his hands." "And also cure that which is healed, and this shall be cauterized with an olive-oil cautery."
"Chapter X."
"On the cautery treatment of epilepsy."
It is not cauterized in the epileptic unless that epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to cleanse the brain at the beginning of the disease and also in the other cure which we have spoken about in the division when the patient has a great deal of phlegm and is tolerant of medicine.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer gargarisms and masticatories to cleanse the throat, previously for several days, with good nutrition.
And afterwards shave the head of the patient, and also cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery in the nape of his neck, and on each horn of the head cauterize also. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with cauteries as mentioned before, having paralysis and relaxation of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And use an olive-shaped cautery according to what has been described previously.
But if the patient is a child, then pause cauterizing him with subtle cauteries in this manner.
Chapter XI
Of cautery for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humidity and thick phlegm, then cauterize the patient with the cauteries that we have mentioned before, having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is superfluous matter declining towards melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then let him drink water that cleanses the brain according to what has been mentioned before in the division.
And also shave the head of the patient, and also make a suitable pigeon's egg of it on the circumference, and also leave that in the middle of the head.
And let the patient lie down on his back, and let him be held from all parts.
And then apply a liniment; and afterwards it is worthy of anointing with wool grease. And heat this over the fire for moderate warming by measure, which the patient can suffer when it is applied to him. And then put this in the middle of the head of the patient within the circle, and let it remain until it becomes cool.
And do this with the patient once each week until it is healed; or if you wish, cauterize him with small punctures according to what suits him best, without having your hand close to a cautery arranged in this way: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And then apply that ointment smeared on wool or on grease of lard.
Chapter XII
"For cauterization of descending water in the heel."
"When you notice the start of water descent in the heel through the signs that I mentioned earlier in this section, then begin and give the patient something to drink which cleanses the head, and protect them from all humidity."
"And do that treatment in a bath for several days."
"And also order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and also cauterize it with two cauteries over both temples if there is the beginning of descent of water in both heels, or on one side if it begins on one heel."
"Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the elbow, and let the cauteries be placed at that distance level with the temples."
"And take care not to have flows of blood when cauterizing. And if you see any flow of blood from it, stop it with some medication, which is within your ability."
"And you should come with skill in extracting arteries and in excising them, and caution against flows of blood."
"Sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below two bones with two cauteries placed perpendicularly."
Chapter XIII.
Of cautery for dried tears.
"When tears from the eyes are abundant and flow through veins and arteries in the visible area of the head, it is verified that this is excess fluid from bloody humors. Therefore, cauterize around the beginning of water entering the eyes with the cautery mentioned at the start of this chapter, and cauterize the middle of the head, two points on either side of the temples, and two points on either side of the jawbones." "If additional cautery is needed, then cauterize the entire length of the eyebrow ridge above the eye with a small cautery."
"Chapter XIV."
"Of cautery for foul odor from the nose."
"When you treat someone with the method described earlier and it does not improve, then prepare and give them to drink the decoction for three nights. After that, shave their head and cauterize the crown of the head with a mixture of cautery iron and olive oil." "And also cauterize the middle of the forehead with clove cautery and two cauteries on either side of the eyebrows where the hair begins, each one a little smaller."
"And be careful not to burn the face with the cautery."
"Here is how to make clove cautery:"
Capital Fifteen
About eyelid cauterization in cases of inflammation.
"When the eyelid is inflamed due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery."
"And if you, cauterize the two eyebrows each with two cauteries in total, and elongated from the temples." "And let the length of each cautery be according to the quantity of the eyebrow."
"And do not press too hard on the cautery with your hand, but according to how much the third part of the eyelid is adhered."
Capítulo XVI
"Regarding cauterizing the eyelid when the hairs turn inward and pierce it."
"When the hairs of the eye turn inward so as to pierce it, there are two ways to cauterize them: either with fire or with a burning medicine."
"If you cauterize with fire, first tell the patient to pluck out his hairs as they are plucked for shaving, until they are lengthened and thickened; and if one of them touches their opening, then immediately close the eyes with a bandage so that it is not harmed until the hairs have grown."
"Then, when they have grown long and thickened, then pause the head of the patient on your thigh, and carefully place the cautery on the eyelid of the eye with the tip, shaped like a grass blade, at the beginning where the hairs are."
"And then place hot wax or dough made from ground barley or pitch on the eye."
"And then light the cautery of which this is the shape:"
"And then cauterize along the shape that you have drawn, little by little, in several views, until the surface of the line that is like a myrtle leaf is completely burned."
"The sign of the completion of this procedure is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and its hairs raised away from the puncture of the eye."
"And then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a border over it until the scar has healed." "And then dress it with ointment until it is healed."
"If some cause returns through the skin near their birthmark and the eyelid is swollen, then apply the cautery again in that same spot just as you did initially."
"If the hairs on the eyelid are growing outwards, then cauterize that area which is elevated to its natural place and sealed so that the hairs cannot stick out."
"If the cautery with medicine burns you, then order the patient to apply the hairs which are lengthened and sealed, and nearby make a bandage of cloth in the shape of a miter leaf."
"And take some known soap and live coal together with a needle, and immediately mix those coals well, and do this with haste so that it does not cool down; then spread that onto the cloth which is made in the shape of a miter leaf, and place it over one eye or both; and place a cloth dipped in egg white over the other eye, keeping the head of the patient upright."
"And place your finger over the medicine and press it gently without moving until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for that bite is like fire."
"And when you perceive that the bite has ceased, then remove the medicine with your finger; and when the bite stops, then rinse the eye with water. Then observe if the eyelid is lifted up as it should be after being washed away by the contraction with fire or incision, for then it will be good. And if not, then place the medicine again on the location where it did not stick previously until it becomes sealed and your operation is completed."
"And then place over that with a border until the chain of adhesion has been eradicated; and then heal it with a poultice or another remedy until it is healed."
"And it is necessary that you, after your operation, carefully guard that nothing falls into the eye from the medicine. And if the eyelid becomes inflamed after several days and some cause of hairs punches into the eye, then repeat your medicine over that location where the eyelid is inflamed; and particularly do this as you did at the beginning until it is healed."
"And this is the shape of a miter leaf."
"And know that the eyes of men are differentiated according to their size and smallness, and therefore your contraction is made accordingly."
"And do not reveal the way of rectitude to one who studies this art."
Chapter XVII.
Of the cauterization of a fistula that is in the lachrymal duct of the eye.
"When you treat fistula as we've said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not conform, then you must cauterize it in this manner: Command the patient to place their head on their knees, and have a servant hold their head between their thighs so that it is not moved nor agitated with their head."
"And then apply heat in the form of hot water or poultice of bird droppings onto the wound."
"Now take the cautery of which this is the shape:"
"That is heated, like the shape of a reed or an eagle's feather, at one end with which you make the cautery."
"And if you wish, it should penetrate somewhat into the other extremity." "And if you want, it should be sharp like a needle."
"But however that which is heated is better for your task."
"And then press the fistula with it if it is open, and draw out from it the sanies (pus) and dry it up." "If it is not open, then apply that cautery there to make it so, and extract the pus from it."
"And then apply the cautery which has been well heated onto it, and with that hand hold it against the wound near the opening of the nostril to prevent your hand or the patient from being burned, and then apply the cautery onto the surface of the wound and destroy it."
"And then if at the beginning of your cautery it enters into the bone, that is good. Otherwise, apply the cautery again once or twice as necessary, and leave it there for three days, and then apply a bandage around it with a drying ointment until it is healed."
"But if after twelve days it has not healed, then apply a caustic medicine to it until the bone is exposed and scrape it according to what you will find in its chapter."
"And another manner of cauterizing a fistula that some first said: Enter into the location of the fistula and make it hot, and then apply there gently with this form: Take as much melted lead as the length of your little finger. And with your hand hold it with good retention so that the molten lead does not fall onto the wound; and cover the wound of the patient with a cloth dipped in album of eggs or water, for the lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it by marvelous healing."
"And if the fistula has been healed by that which we have said about cauterization and cure, then that is good. Otherwise, it will be necessary to administer a probe through the nostril and reduce the fistula into the nasal cavity according to what will come in its proper place."
Chapter XVIII
On cauterizing the split in the buttocks.
"Fissures occur frequently in the lips, especially in those of infants."
"So when you treat these fissures with what we have said in the divisions and it does not help, then immediately use a small cauterizing scalpel according to this form that is indicated:"

Chapter XIX.
Of the cautery of a fistula in the mouth
When in the root of the uvula or on the palate or on the roots of the teeth an aposteme appears and it grows, and then bursts, and becomes ancient, and blood flows and a fistula is formed, and you treat that and it does not benefit from treatment, then it is necessary for you to heat cautery according to the amount received in the fistula, and put that heated one into the passage of the fistula, and keep your hand there until the hot iron reaches the bottom of it, and at the end of it do this once or twice; and then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.

Chapter XX
Of the cautery of teeth and softening of the gums
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are painful, and you treat them with medicines but they do not improve, then place the head of the sick one on the chair, and heat the cautery in this form which will come later, so that you put the pincers over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, keeping your hand there a little while until the sick one feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand and repeat the cautery several times according to what you want.
And then the sick one should rinse his mouth with salt water and hold it there for an hour, and afterwards spit that out, because the painful tooth is made firm and the softened gum is strengthened and the corrupt moisture is dried.

Chapter XXXVI
Regarding the cauterization of fistulas naturally occurring in the skin.
If nature does not follow the treatment recommended here and the patient becomes frightened as a result, then apply a cautery to heal it.
Therefore, when there is some fistula appearing naturally on the skin that has become old and corrupted by dryness and moisture, then it is more appropriate to probe it with a thin, subtle thread and thus determine its depth using the aforementioned thread.
Then heat the cautery which resembles a ray. Then insert that heated cautery into this fistula, following the depth indicated by the thread.
And continue applying the cautery until those corrupt tissues are completely burned away in one or two or three applications, depending on the severity of the condition.
But be careful not to burn any nerves or veins. And if the fistula goes into the bladder or the intestines, then be very careful in those areas. Do not do this unless the fistula is in fatty tissue and you believe it is not penetrating deeply.
Then dress the wound until it heals.
And if the opening becomes sealed and the infected material is removed from it, and it remains like this for a long time, then you will know that it has healed completely.
And if the infected material is not coming out of it, then you should know that it is penetrating or that there is some underlying bone corruption. This may be due to one of those causes which will be remembered later in this chapter.

Oh, son, this kind of cauterization we have rarely used due to the fear of the wound's appearance, and because few patients we have found who tolerate it.
But among those who endured them, some had that mark on their leg.

Then press it gently with your hand for good compression, and allow the boiled water to drain from the pierced hole into the inverted pot underneath.

"Chapter XLV."

"And the patient should lie on his back for forty days until the area and wound are completely healed."
"And it is necessary that you regulate the patient's diet according to the length of time for his cure which will empty his belly, and that the bone does not come out due to the effort of healing."
"And when he wants to get up after forty days, then administer a suitable plaster and keep him for another forty days."
"And let him be spared from work and from eating and drinking in abundance and from violent coughing, because when he uses this complete regimen of healing he will not be healed."
"And I will tell you the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the belly, and if they are large at the beginning, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its quantity, as much as it is protruded from the anus like the third section of it; and then treat it with what has been said, and nothing else."
"And some earlier authors have said that rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery like this figure:"
"And the line is placed that they pass through the width in the side up from under the rupture, and another line above; then place your hand with a pointed cautery, but the first cautery should be lighter and better."
"The forty-sixth chapter"
"Of cauterization of the groin."
"When pain occurs in the upper limbs due to chafing or percussion and persists for a long time without improving with treatment using medications, it is then necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery."
"And the cautery should be of such size according to the magnitude and smallness of the member, and its virtue and power, and the location of the hernia and the pain."
"So if it is healed by the first cautery, that's good; but if not, then apply the cautery again; for those pains are usually caused by pressure on a member near the one in question, so it is necessary to reach those with cautery until the illness is healed."
"The forty-seventh chapter"
"Of cauterization of leprosy."
"Some lepers have been viewed as cauterized with little benefit and even harm if the type of leprosy is caused by decay of phlegm and melancholy."
"And therefore, when you want to cauterize that person, consider whether the leprosy is contagious and whether it has been cured with what we have said in the division above. If not, then be afraid that the corruption will spread throughout his entire body and complexion. Therefore, cauterize him on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, another towards the end of the forehead near the hair; two on both horns; and one behind, on the nape or top of the neck."
"Finally, press your hand with the cautery a little deeper until it impresses slightly on the bone, and remove the scabs that have formed there, so that the expiration of thick vapors may be alleviated from him; and cauterize on the chest as is necessary."
"And if the leprosy has already spread over the person and appears manifestly, then you must cauterize that person with those cauteries which we have mentioned for the head; and cauterize on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on both cheeks, and with two cauteries on both shoulder blades, and with six cauteries on the spine of the back, and with one large cautery on the spine near the buttocks, and one on the buttock itself, and with two on the ankles, and one on each ankle, and with two on the knees and one on each knee, and with two on the elbows and two on the muscles of the calves; and cauterize him on all the joints of the said limbs and feet, and on each heel of his feet, and on the hollows of his hands."
"And beware not to burn those nerves which are situated on the heel lest you should injure them."
"Sometimes it is necessary to cauterize with one cautery on the bone of the sternum and another on the mouth of the stomach and another on the liver."
"And know that the more closely you apply the cauteries, the greater is the benefit and efficacy; and know that the person does not feel pain from the cautery as he would from his own sores, because his body has become numb."
"And it is necessary that the cauteries be large or small according to the limbs and joints as is prescribed by the nature of the instruments."
"And afterwards, dress the wound with fine bran flour mixed with honey and with another dressing until it heals."
"Chapter XLVIII"
"Of cautery for swelling."
"If any limb is paralyzed or numb and is treated with medications, oils, and plasters, but does not heal, then cauterize that paralyzed limb with a cautery according to the size of the limb or its smallness."
"And insert your cautery into the spine through an incision." And then care for him until he is healed.
Sometimes, certain wounds are cauterized which affect the hands and feet, the spine of the back and waist, and the nerves that move those limbs. The wound is then healed.
Only let those presume to do this who are wise in anatomy of the members and in the exit of nerves that move all the body.
Capítulo XLIX
On cauterizing warts that have been removed.
When the ulcer is ancient and does not respond to some medical remedies, then cauterize it with a pointed cautery according to the quantity of the specificity of the cord, until its whiteness and color are removed, and its color is changed.
And appropriate for this is to cure it with flour of barley and lentils mixed with olive oil and leaves of sage, and with dove's blood or hen's blood, giving each one a portion and mixing all together, and then placing it on a cloth on the affected area until it is healed.
Chapter One
On the cautery of a boil.
When a boil begins and you wish to open it, then cauterize around its circumference with a circular cautery in the circuit of it thus as it turns.
And the wise say that it should be cauterized with the last cautery in the middle of it; but I have not seen this, because I fear that it may become ulcerated, and I have seen it happen sometimes.
And it is more correct to cauterize around its circumference with a circular cautery as we have said above, or with several cauteries.
Chapter Two:
"Regarding the cautery of whitened skin."
"If some skin whitening is delayed in maturation, this may be due to superfluity produced by that (area), or because of the condition of the patient, who is elderly and decrepit with little blood, or because of time, and you wish to expedite the maturation of whitening; therefore cauterize around it with a small pointed cautery iron; and leave that (area) until it has matured."
"But if you wish to perforate with the cautery, then heat the cautery of which this is the shape: and place it in the middle of the whitening where the chain penetrates."
"And if you cauterize that (area) which is lower down, so that the flesh of the arm may be alleviated, and then treat it until it has healed."
"Chapter LII."
"On the cautery of ulcers."
"Ulcers are corrupting wanderings in the member, which consume that as dry wood consumes fire."
"Therefore if you cauterize that ulcer where it can withstand fire cautery, then heat cautery needles few and large according to what is suitable in that place where the ulcer is."
"And then cauterize it from beginning to end until you have completely eradicated all corruption and there remains no cause of it."

"And I have already spoken of the treatment of ulcers with sharp medicine in their division, and therefore take that when you begin to encounter them."
"Chapter LIII."
"On the cautery of chilblains and similar conditions."
"Very often in the lower parts of the leg there appears a disease called chilblain, which is caused by cold and dampness, making the flesh numb. And it is treated in two ways: cautery with fire and cautery with hot water."
"But cautery with fire is done by heating the cautery concave similarly to a vulture's feather, which should be made of iron according to the quantity that the chilblain encompasses from beginning to end, and it should have thin edges."
"And then place that heated cautery upon the chilblain and then rotate your hand with the cautery around the circumference of the chilblain until it reaches the depth of it." "Then leave it for three days until it begins to swell." "And then apply a plaster of wild mallows pounded with salt, and leave the plaster on it overnight, because that will eradicate its roots."
"And then treat the place with flesh-generating ointment until it is healed."
"And if it is not healed, and many times this occurs on the surface of the body, more often in the thighs and legs, then you should administer cautery with hot water there. And that is done by taking a cannula of brass or iron or vulture's feather and placing it upon the chilblain; and then pour some hot water into the cannula and hold your hand, and rotate that with compression from above so that the edge of the cannula presses upon the root of the chilblain and the hot water flows to the front of the root of the chilblain. And wait for the pain of the hot water for about an hour, and then remove it, because the chilblain along with its roots will be eradicated."
"And do this to those likewise until all of them upon which they have appeared on the body are removed."
"And then treat those with ointments that cause flesh to regenerate since the roots of the chilblains have been eradicated."

"And trembling will cease and the health of the cold disease will be restored."

"Chapter two."
When there occur cataracts or ulcers in the eyes or breast, and these are ancient, and cure cannot be effected by medicines, then the last remedy in this case is incision of those two arteries.
And thus you must do when you wish to incise those arteries which the sick person's head has near to it, and first rub that place with rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and also bind the neck of the sick person with some cloth; and consider well where the vein pulses; and the places for cutting on each side are over the places which are near the ears below, and they are hidden except in certain men.
And then mark with a thread the artery you wish to cut, and then incise each one with a scalpel of sharp point, making an incision entering into the bone, and let this be done along the breadth of the head.

Chapter III
Of the extraction of those arteries which are in the temples.
When it happens that some ancient migraine or very severe headache occurs due to humid and hot qualities in the temples, or if it is an ancient vehement disease similar to these, and it is treated with the usual methods of medical cure without success, then we have proven in these diseases extraction of those arteries in the temples, or cauterization of them as we have shown.
And the manner of operation and extraction of those arteries is this: Shave the hair from those which are in the temples; and understand that the artery appears on the temple where it is indicated by its pulsation, although it may be hidden in few men due to great thickness of flesh.
And if it is hidden from you, then let the neck of the disease be strengthened with some cloth; and then rub the place with a rough cloth or evaporate the place with hot water until the artery appears clearly to you. And then take this scalpel which has this shape: And then cut the skin with that one easily until you reach the artery; and then open it there and extract that upwards to such an extent that you extract it from its sheaths and uncover those tunics which surround it on all sides.
But if the artery is delicate, then wrap it with the extreme end of a probe; and then cut one part of it according to how much its extremities are elongated and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they are not incised the blood will not come forth.
And then let out of it one sixth part or at most three parts of blood.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with double strong thread of cotton or long cords, so that putrefaction does not occur before the wound is consolidated and there is no flow of blood.
And then incise the superfluity which is between two lint layers; and do this at that hour or another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a caustic cautery or cutlery cautery so that its extremities are incised, and this will take the place of that operation.
And it is better to operate if the patient has fever or is of hot complexion, because cauterization helps those causes that promote humidifying qualities, and therefore it is better in this case.
And it is necessary that in the extraction of arteries the place of cutting be smeared with good ointment, and that they be powdered decently with dry medicines to make the flesh grow, and then it should be cured with dressings until it is healed.
And if while operating another time, blood begins to flow from the artery, then stop that blood either with caustic or with compressing the place of cutting, and strengthen your hand until the blood is stopped. And if none of those causes is present, then place your finger on the place until the blood coagulates, and then bore the place with cold water until the sharpness passes, and then strengthen it according to what is necessary.
And that which is put lightly in extraction of artery is this: You cauterize the vein with this caustic cautery having two cuts, for you have cut both places with a probe, and then ignite each one separately, so that they reach the bone and incise the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities may be elongated, because it is not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the caustic:
And let the sharpness of the two cuts be thus But if they are not very sharp, then it is better to have two cuts, for if they are very sharp with two cuts near each other, they approach too close and do not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held within them and the flesh is easily cut.
And this operation is better than all others and is also easier.

"Chapter .vi"
Of treating that which falls into the ears.

Chapter VII
Of the treatment of earache.
Sometimes, some children come out of their mothers' wombs with closed anuses, and this has happened to some men, causing earache because of phlegm or flesh stuck there; sometimes the blockage is in the opening of the auditory canal which does not allow for sound, and sometimes it is outside at the openings.
And that which happens in the front of it, which does not admit sound, is among the most difficult to cure conditions.
"And in that one where you succeed in finding the vein, then place your ear on the patient's solar plexus and listen." "Therefore if there is apparent oppression, open that small space with a subtle spatula whose form is like this:"
"For the end of it where its width is small, one part is pointed and the other is not; and let the spatula be of two lion's teeth in such a way that it does not harm the ear."
"And if the oppression is due to skin grown over the ear or its membrane, then use those subtle tweezers and carefully cut that violent inflammation so as to remove all the skin."
"And if oppression is in the entrance of the ear, then take a thin lion's ray and heat it slightly over fire, and carefully place that within the flesh of the ear."
"And therefore if you find oppression with that which the patient draws with facility, for the gravity of his hearing, ease that oppression with facility; and take care not to plague the nerves of the ear."
"And carefully insert into the ear drops of it according to the quantity of the width of the wick which you have inserted in the Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the consolidation of the plug; or that you take some wax and insert that into ears and powder over it thrice and use it; and if sometimes the ear is already apostematous, then it is necessary that you extract the wax and replace it with another wax which you will envelop in cerot made with rose oil until the apostema is cauterized; and carefully cure it until it is healed."
"But if it happens that there is a flow of blood, then submerge some linen or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other cure which you restrict the flow of blood, which is called in the treatise of distillation."
"Chapter VIII."
"Treatment for eye inflammation that occurs in eyelids."
"Eyelid inflammation sometimes causes tearing, sometimes they are dry."
"You should hold those hands while having them raised or lowered, and then carefully separate those hands from their roots, and slowly lower them onto the places indicated."
"If there is pain with blood flow, then cauterize those with a fine antimonial cautery."
"And the best cauterization is when the incisions are made and often retaken until there remains no cause for their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return."
Chapter IX
"Treatment for swelling that comes to the eyelids."
Often, swelling occurs in the eyelids due to some cause which is similar to hardness and toughness, and for this reason it is called hardness.
"And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the left eyelid above."
Therefore, the operation in this case is that you should consider whether the swelling appears on the outside of the eyelid and is swollen all over; and its cause will be light.
Therefore, make an incision along the width of that [swelling], and then carefully cut it open all around with ease, and then raise it up and sever it.
And if the swelling is large, then you should adjust it with stitches; and cure it until it is healed.
But if it is small, no harm will come to that one because the ointment for that restores it and makes it grow again.
And if the swelling declines towards the inside of the eyelid, then you should lift up the eyelid and suspend the swelling with a hook, unless you make an incision; press it all around, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, then it will be known for no cause.
And after making the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and cure that place with something that makes it grow again until it is healed.
Chapter X.
On the treatment of inflammation in the upper eyelid.
"Eyelid inflammation is common, especially in children, causing eye irritation and difficulty seeing due to excessive tearing."
"Therefore, these individuals often rub their eyes and face while awake and asleep. Their eyelids are swollen with excessive moisture, which is already covered by inflammation. There is a visible indication of infection in the eye. When you press on this location with your fingers, you can feel the pulsating heat."
"Some method of operation is required for those who pause their head on the side they are facing, draping themselves and their foot over it. First, measure the circumference according to the quantity of earthworm present in each section. Then place the circle around that area and press firmly with your fingers all around so as to accumulate pus in the middle of the circle. Finally, make an incision with a scalpel along the width, ensuring that the incision is not larger than that made during phlebotomy."
"When opening the head wound, ensure that you reach all the way to the pus, as in many cases it will ooze out through the incision like a white, viscous fluid."
"Then remove those drapes which you had wrapped around the parts using your fingers and shake them between your right and left hand until all pus has been shaken out."
"And be careful not to insert the scalpel too deep into the incision, lest you pierce the eye or cause injury."
"But if pus does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then insert the scalpel gently into the incision until pus begins to flow out. Then remove it as we have described. Next, rinse drapes in water and vinegar and apply them to the location, and finally powder those drapes."
"And some also use salt and apply it to the concavity of the incision so that any remaining pus may be drawn out."
"And finally, dress the wound until it is healed."
"But if you encounter an abscess in the location, then treat it with cataplasms made of leadwort until it heals."
Chapter XI
"Of ways to examine the eye."
When the eyelids are born with defects due to improperly grown lashes, then they affect the eye and cause diseases such as constant tears, inflammation of the eyelids, and damage to the eye tissue, which is called entropion of the eye. There are four methods of treatment: either by cauterization with fire; or with acute medication as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization; or it is done through incision and suturing; or with canthotomy as I have said before.
Then gently lift the patient's head towards them; next, with their left hand, they should turn the eyelid of the affected eye inward. If it turns inwards properly, that is good. But if not, then insert a needle into that part of the eyelid where the lashes grow and make the needle penetrate upwards close to the edge of the eyelid where the lashes grow. Then pull the thread out from above the eyelid and turn that eyelid inward with their finger.
And next, make an incision in the middle of the eyelid just below the lashes with a scalpel entering the larger lacrimal gland into the smaller one. Then remove the thread and pause near the eyelid using small cotton swabs or cloth, and then press on the eyelid with your finger so that it forms like a leaf of myrtle.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of eversion of the eyelid, because this is already varied among individuals. Therefore, some people require that a larger portion of the eyelid be incised according to the amount by which the eyelid has turned outwards.
And next, make an incision with the scalpel along both lines that you have marked out, starting from the larger lacrimal gland and entering into the smaller one. If your incision is close to the natural lashes so that it matches the thickness of the reed (i.e., not too deep).
And then take forceps and grasp one of the two ends of the thread. Then cut off all of it, and next join together both lips of the wound using silk or fine wool thread and cleanse away any blood. Then make what is superfluous from the threads adhere to both eyebrows with some adhesive substance if you wish to do so; otherwise, do not bother.
And then leave the suture of the threads in place for three or four days, and then remove it and care for the wound.
And if you wish, leave the plaster on without removing the suture and care for it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is elevated due to the sealing of the plaster and its adherence. But sewing is better. This is what the ancient authorities say.
However, if there is concern about the illness, it is good to operate, and there is no reason to fear doing so.
And there is another method of sewing, which is to mark out on the eyelid a figure like a leaf of myrtle, and then raise the eyelid using three forceps separated and gathered together in this shape:
But if this is not possible, then hold the forceps and do not let go of them. Then take a needle with thread inserted into it and put it in the middle of the figure. Then insert one end of the thread near the larger lacrimal gland and the other end near the smaller lacrimal gland. Then adjust both ends of the threads equally between your fingers, and then raise your hand while lifting them up gently so that the entire thread marked out is raised as we have recommended.
And then join together both lips of the plaster with a suture, and care for it until it has healed.
But if an abscess forms over your incision or wound, cut into the abscess with a scalpel and drain it, and apply a poultice.
And sometimes it happens that the hairs of the eyelid are turned inward. In this case, you should use the method we have described for incision; but stitching in each case is more artful.
To stitch with needles, do it like this: "Now you must turn the eyelid and cut the figure that lies beneath, according to what has been described; then with art make two tubes or two thin needles whose length is equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of the spatula." "And now you have already cut into the ends of each half, where you hold the threads."
"Next, gently bring together what is superfluous of the eyelid on both sides and press the tubes of each half with great firmness; leave it thus for a few days so that the thread may die, be rejected, and fall out by itself."
"But if it is delayed, then cut it with the scissors; next clean the wound until it heals."
"So when it has healed, the eyelid will be raised and the hairs will not touch (the eye)."
"And this is how you make the two tubes which you should make according to this form:" "and they should have exactly the same length and width, neither more nor less."
Chapter XII
"Of raising hairs that are sticking into the eye when the hair is hard"
"When the hairs are not natural, hard and sharp and sticking into the eyes, then the operation is this: you should take a fine needle and insert it under that fine thread of cedar and lion's hair, and carefully adjust its ends without hurting each one in any way." "And let the length of the threads be as much as half a palm (about 7.6 cm)."
"And next take another fine thread and tie it around that one if you wish."
"And then place the patient's head on a suitable support and keep it near the light so that your work may be clear, for this operation is subtle and precise."
"And next insert the needle into the root of the hair that needs to be removed and make it penetrate into the eyelid quickly until you pull out that one from above the natural hair."
"And then pull out the hair and insert this one in its place; and let there be one, two or three hairs at most, not more."
"And next pull your hand away from that until the hair is completely pulled out with its root in the eyelid along with the natural hair."
"But if it is pulled out but not completely removed with its root, then pull gently from both sides using the thread that you have made with it until it is completely removed with its root underneath."
"And then examine the eyelid with the eye because then you will pull out that thread and knot each one and close the eyelid, leaving it like that for two or three days until the hair adheres to its place and grows over good flesh."
"And if the hair is too short, then adjust a long natural hair and raise it with that one or let it grow for some space until it grows longer, and then raise that."
Chapter XIII
"Of treating the pain that comes in the eyelid from above."
"When the eyelid suffers from pain, it is due to the contraction of the eyelid when it contracts upwards, on either side, due to poor healing of a wound or cut or burn, and this is called 'leporina', which may be natural or accidental, caused by poor healing of wounds or cuts or burns, or by resembling those who are ill."
"The manner of operating on this is to divide that contraction and separate the eyelid's lips, and place in it a wick of linen soaked in water, and tie those together until they are healed."
"And it is not necessary to use the treatments that dry and constrict because if you do that, it returns to causing pain worse than before."
"Use on that causes moistness such as fenugreek, and soak it in water with cooking salt and honey or diacylon, which is melted with some oil, and immerse the wick in it and treat with that."
"And the treasure of your treatment is to persevere with all your strength until nothing remains unhealed as at the beginning."
"And this operation that we have described is only a slight correction of eyelid inflammation that does not restore its original form."
Chapter XIV
"Of treating the pain that occurs in the outer corner of the eyelid."
"This inflammation that occurs in the outer corner of the eyelid is called 'ptosis' properly speaking. It may be due to some natural cause or other accidental causes."
"Accidental causes include wounds, cuts, burns, and similar afflictions."
"The method of operating on this is to take a needle with double thread and insert it into the flesh, passing from the left lacrimal duct to the right one until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh."
"And then extend the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with the needle, and incise with a broad scalpel." "And then if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, that is good. But if not, take the thread and place it in the cut shape, and with that turn the eyelid over and close both incisions on the outer side of the eyelid."
"And let both ends of the incisions meet at two angles of the incision that you have made, so that when they come together they form an angle with a well-shaped figure."
"And this figure is the Greek letter lambda (λ)."
"And then remove that flesh on the side where it tapers towards the outer corner of the eyelid."
"And then adjust the separated parts and sew them together with thread, and then treat them with moisturizing medicines and bandages until they are healed."
"And if inflammation occurs due to a cut or suture or burn, then it is necessary to make an incision with a scalpel over the hairs of the eyelid as described previously." "And then separate with forceps between the two lips as we have said before."
"In summary of the speech on treating inflammation when it is above or below, follow the operation according to how the inflammation is prepared, and that is in many diverse forms."
"And the wise artisan always prepares various methods for treating inflammation in every possible way to restore its natural form or close to it, according to what suits that condition and the operation and instruments used in the best conditions."
Chapter XV
"Of adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or the cornea."
"When this adhesion occurs in many people such that the eyelid is stuck above with a film of mucus and movement is difficult and natural functions are prevented, then it is necessary to insert the outer end of the thread under the eyelid and lift it up or extend it with forceps."

"Chapter XVI."
Of the incision of a nail and the excision of flesh from the lacrimals.
Know that a nail is of two kinds: one is nerve-like, and resembles hard cartilage; the other is non-nerve-like, resembling thickened white humor, which, when touched by iron, may be forcibly severed without the iron entering it.
And both species begin only at the larger lacrimal, which gradually decreases until it covers the sight and prevents light and its movement from passing through it.
But the manner of operating in the incision of it is that the patient should raise his head towards the ceiling, and widely open his eyes, and lift up the eyelid with your hand, and carefully take hold of the nail with forceps and extend it upwards. And then take a needle and insert it under the nail a little way with horsehair or cowhair or oxhair or similar hair, or strong thread, and fold over the tip of the needle bit by bit and insert it into the middle of the nail, and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it upwards, and cut away with the hair the side of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you should follow that with the hair behind it.
And then you should incise the remnant in the root of the lacrimal with a fine spatula or small forceps, and lift up the natural cartilage of the lacrimal so that by cutting into it there would be no continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail with its edge extended or tied with thread, as we have said, and then it should be cut away with this kind of spoon:
"Be mindful so that with the scalpel you do not touch the corneal tunic and that it ruptures, causing the pupil to be perforated."

"If the nail is not attached and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it fixed in that place, then sometimes this type of nail can be cured with medicines, and it will heal like the rest."
"If you wish to incise there, then first open the wound of the disease and take a scalpel made from lion's claw with sharpness according to this form:" "with subtlety." "And if it happens that it is rarified and congealed and sticks in that way, then distill some of your saliva into the wound with clean oil of sinapis or with rose oil or with crushed salt, and cover the wound overnight."
"And again perform the operation on it until all that belongs to it is removed, so that instead of a tumor there may be an ulcer in the wound, because sometimes when you leave it alone, the tumor will begin to heal."
"And again perform your operation on it with reason until it is healed."
"The membrane of the lachrymal gland, if it is noticed in the wound causing great harm, should be suspended with a clamp. Then cut into it there through one opening only, and do not extend the incision lest tear fluid flow out."
"And again distill rose oil and oil of sinapis into the lachrymal gland until it is healed."
"Chapter XVII."
"Of Incision of an Abscess and of That Which Is Born from Adherent Flesh in the Wound."
A lump of flesh may sometimes be born in the eyes of some individuals, and it is pressed upon and covers the pupil, or approaches or presses upon the eyelids, forcing them open from outside, appearing like a ball of ivory.
The operation in this case is that the patient places their head on the operating table; and then carefully opens the eyes of that person and collects all that flesh with suitable scissors to that end, taking it with a forceps or with tweezers; and then cuts the flesh at its beginning so that it is entirely finished with the scissors, guarding yourself from the eye not to let anything fall during your operation.
And let your incision be that with which you cut those [tissues], or use the spatula we have mentioned in the incision of the nail, or small forceps with which you collect what comes afterwards according to the form that this chapter will explain.
And when your incision is completed and all that flesh has been removed, then fill the eye with finely crushed salt or distilled into powder for rogues and similar to the medicines corrosives, and cover the eye from outside with an egg white until it is assured of cauterization.
And if there remains some cause of inflammation and the blood is not completely cauterized, and you fear cauterization apostema, then leave the eye alone and treat that apostema with what will ease it, and then repeat this operation until it is healed; and similarly do for the flesh adhered to the eye in this condition.
But you must ensure that all care of the eye is done naturally or accidentally without using iron instruments, and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that member has recovered from that operation.

"Chapter XIX."
"For the reduction of deviation of the nose."

Chapter XX
On the reduction of prominence of the brow.
When the brow projects excessively and there is no disfigurement or loss of sight resulting from it, then it is necessary to alleviate this condition by following this procedure: first, perform scarifications and cupping on the back of the neck for good suction; then apply a liniment made of aloe, myrrh, and tragacanth gum over the brow, followed by applying an emplaster made of quicklime powder.
"And the way to prepare this powder is to take some quicklime and fold each piece of cloth into four layers. Then make the powder from this."
"And then apply these powders one over another according to the amount needed for the brow's prominence." "And then apply a hard cloth with strong compression over these powders. Then perform cupping again with suction without scarification. Finally, remove the cups and leave the brow bandaged day and night."
"But if during this time the bandage becomes loose, then it must be tightened."
"And remove the bandage day and night as mentioned earlier."
"And if the brow is reduced enough, that's good. But if not, then repeat the application of the plaster with powders, compression and cupping until it is healed."

Capital XXII
Of the treatment of sciatica
This condition called sciatica is nothing but pain that runs along the leg like descending water, not the one above.
To treat this condition, have the patient sit on a prepared chair and lean their head back into your hands. Then find where the pain is coming from and stop it from spreading further down the leg. Then cover them with a blanket and let them rest in that position so they can see what you're doing.
And if it does not descend any further, know that it is water.
But if the flow of pain has not been directed downwards as previously described, then have the patient sit between your palms and take a thin spatula to press gently on the skin above the connective tissue around the junction of the conjunctiva and tunica cornea at the crown (i.e. the area indicated) until the pain is released.
Once you have released the pain, then mix honey with water or with boiled fenugreek and honey water to make a caudate mixture.
And also treat it with other causes until it is healed.
**Chapter XXII.**
About treating water descent from the head.
We have already spoken of the types of urine in division and which is convenient to perforate, indicating it according to health.
Then you should make the patient sit between your thighs on a chair and hold their head down with your hands tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their left eye if it is the left ear where the water is, or place your right hand there if it is the right ear.
And then with your right hand take a bandage if it is the left ear, or with your left hand if it is the right ear.
And then place the end of the bandage near the crown, as thick as a ray from the ear towards the inner corner of the eye, and then gently press the bandage with your hand over it.
And then penetrate into the ear canal until you feel that the bandage has reached the eardrum.
The length of the bandage should be measured from the front to match the distance between the pupil and the end of the blackness in the ear canal, which is the crown of the ear; because you will see the pupil through the clarity of the ear drum.
And then place the bandage in the ear canal where the water is, and then quickly pull it out once or twice. If the water drains immediately afterwards, then the patient's hearing should improve immediately, and the bandage will be wet from the earwax.
And then wait a moment; if the water has drained, then place another bandage in the ear canal, but be careful not to push it too far.
And then if it does not drain, carefully remove the bandage and gently rotate it a little bit inside the ear canal to try to break up any wax. Then dissolve a small amount of table salt in some warm water and use that to rinse out the ear canal. But those who are from our time, pause over that command with a sigh of relief.

And then carefully insert the curette into that junction, and do not push your hand too far into the ear canal. And if you cannot make a way for the bandage, do not proceed further.
Then gently insert the bandage as explained earlier.
Before beginning your work, ensure that the patient is lying down comfortably on their side in a dark room where they can remain still without moving.
Ensure that the water you are using is clear and does not contain any impurities or contaminants, and do not wet the patient's hair on either side of their head.
And ensure that the bandage is well positioned according to its shape on the third day.
And then remove that bandage in the dark room and expose the patient's ear canal to fresh air. Allow them to see some light for a short time, then return the bandage to its position on the seventh day.
You should not perform this procedure during the hours of fasting or immediately after taking medication. Be sure to do this gently and carefully, as water can easily enter too quickly and forcefully into the ear canal with great regard for the patient's comfort.
But if an abscess forms, then you should remove the bandage on the seventh day and adjust it with something that will help drain the abscess.
Then uncover the ear and place a dressing over the face of the opening to direct the discharge away from the eye for several days while keeping the patient in the dark room.
And then gradually remove that bandage and explain the causes to them.
Know that even in the classroom, it is not always possible for a student to observe everything at times, and therefore you should be careful about performing this operation.
