"Of incisions of veins, there are two kinds."
"Veins are varicose, and there are also large veins that are convoluted and filled with melancholic superfluities, which appear on the surface of the limbs of the body, and their greatest occurrence is in the thighs, especially those of travelers, laborers, and porters."
"Therefore you should administer purgation of the body with black bile several times with strong evacuation, and also perform bloodletting from the patient's basilica vein."
"But the cure of these (varicose veins) with iron is done in two ways: one way is to incise and draw out that black blood, and the other way is to raise the vein and remove all of it."
"The section is done in this way: first wet the thigh with cold water to dissolve the thick blood that is like mud from the thigh. Then tie up the thigh from the affected area near its upper end down to below the knee joint with a bandage, and then incise the vein at one place or two places or three places with a wide incision, and then elevate the black blood from below the thigh upwards with the thumb of your left hand from above it downwards until you draw out as much blood as you want according to how much is enough, which relieves the power of the ailment."
"And then tie it up, and tell him to abstain from foods that generate black bile."
"And repeat the cure with phlebotomy when the veins are filled again, and let the patient know about this."
"But the elevation of these (varicose veins) is done in this way: first shave the thigh from below where they are swollen, and then put it in warm water and wet the thigh with cold water until it becomes hot and the veins are engorged; or have it exercised by vigorous exercise if warm water is not available, so that the member may be warmed up."
"And then cut across the cord at the right of the vein with an incision along its length or towards its end near below the knee joint, or on its lower side near the ankle."
"And then bind the cord with forceps and strip away the vein on all sides until it becomes apparent to touch, and it should appear very dark in color when incised."
"And then remove the cord and you will find a white thread that is like string."
"And then put your left hand under it so that it may be raised up and removed from the cord, and suspend it with one hook of gold."
"And then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth and strip away the cord from above the vein until it becomes apparent."
"And then elevate it and make another incision near the ankle joint, and extract that through the previous section until it is extracted through the second section, and also extract it from the section that is above it."
"And do this until you extract it from the section that is above or altogether from all sections until all of it is extracted, and then close up the incision."
"And if you are unable to extract and elevate it, then insert a strong double-threaded needle with thread through the vein and tie it up and extract it, or put your hand inside and turn it around on all sides until it is removed." Be cautious that it does not slip; for if it breaks, it becomes difficult to raise that matter and it comes upon the patient through that injury.
Once all the air is expelled, then pause on the location of the wound submerged in wine and oil (both refined and unrefined), and care for that injury until it is healed.
If the vein is contracted and there are tortuous parts where there should be no bending, and it does not follow rectitude as we have said, then you must cut over that entire bent part of it and at the locations of those bends, and carefully suspend that with a hook until you raise that entire (vein).
And be careful with your section so as not to slip the vein and not injure it, for it will be difficult to raise that (vein); and use your discretion in this.
**Forma de l'incident amb la qual es talla la vena:**
"It is not like this with hooks, nor does it have an acute extremity so that it does not wound the vein; and it has rough flexibility, because if it were subtle, it would tear the vein due to its subtlety; indeed, it has excess thickness as we said."
"On extracting a vein from the leg."
"This vein is generated in the chambers of dry lands, such as those found in Arabic lands and the dry lands of India that are lacking in moisture and tenderness, where there is neither grass nor herbs."
"And this vein is also generated elsewhere in the body besides the chambers."
"And its generation is of putrefaction, which comes from leaving the spine like venom inside the body, producing serpents, worms, and crabs, as well as other vermin generated between the spine and the flesh."
"And the herald of the arrival of this vein is an inflammation that occurs in the chamber with violent swelling; and nearby, the skin becomes blistered; and near where it begins to come out from that blister, it seems like a root of a plant."
"And then, when the extremity appears, immediately you should wrap around it a lead weight of which the weight is that of one and a half drachmas, hanging that and leaving the lead suspended from the chamber."
"And whenever some cause makes it necessary to move the vein externally, wrap that in lead and fasten it." "And if it is prolonged too much, then you should incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; but do not incision that until it has come out completely outside, because if you incision it while it is still inside, it will be contracted and drawn into the flesh, causing inflammation and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration."
"Therefore, it must be treated gradually and extracted little by little until it is completely outside of the body, so that there remains no cause for it within the body."
"And this vein can be as much as two and twenty palms in length in some people, and I have heard of one that was thirty palms."
"And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into the track of it and pierce that with a long probe through the body until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and administer putrefaction to the place for several days with medicines; and also cure it by the treatment of apostemes."
"And sometimes this vein has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot or in the foot, and it comes to have many openings, each branch having its own opening."
"And then treat it as we have said in the division above, with what has been said about it."
"Regarding the cutting of worms that are generated under the skin and are called 'bovine sickness'."
"This sickness is called in some lands 'bovine sickness' because many times it occurs in oxen and it is a worm that is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes through the whole body pushing and pulling, making itself felt by its movement from member to member until it breaks the skin and comes out."
"And the generation of this [worm] is due to putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, serpents, earthworms, and roundworms are generated in the belly."
"And it is feared because of the malice of this [worm], for when it goes through the body and rises to the head, reaching the eye, then it causes great inflammation there and enters into it, destroying the eye, which happens many times."
"And therefore when you want to treat this [worm] and extract it, then you must follow its movement and appearance by feeling it, and then grasp what is above it and below it with a ligature or a bandage, and dry that area and extract it."
"And if it is stuck in the flesh and cannot be grasped, then apply a cautery with fire to the place where it resides until it is destroyed, for the greatest danger is that it will corrupt there, as we have said."
"If you want to treat it when it has reached the head near the eye, then grasp above the eyebrow with a tight bandage and dry that area, and extract it; and the patient should also use medicines to purge his body of corrupt humors, and avoid foods that cause putrefaction."
"Regarding the cutting for the sickness called 'nafrid'."
"This sickness is called in our land 'nafrid', and it is a pain that comes to certain members, especially moving from member to member."
"And you have seen this before, as I will tell you." "I was called to visit a woman who was ill in a certain town, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a small inflammation on the vein of the elbow, and after an hour had passed, I saw that inflammation move like a worm pushing towards that place, but more gently than if it were actually moving, as if it were living silver passing from place to place."
"And then the pain came to that place and settled in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour."
"And then it ran through the other body until it reached the other arm."
"And she told me that it had turned around in her whole body as I had seen, and she was greatly amazed by its changeability and speed from member to member." "And I had seen this before in my own eyes according to that manner, but I had rarely seen such pain moving from member to member." "But I have not seen anything like this with my own eyes as I did with the woman."
"And I do not think that it was anything other than this because the woman was elderly, with a dry body and visible veins, and in this appearance at the sensation there was that wandering windiness."
"And it is necessary that this [sickness] does not appear according to this retrogression in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins."
"And when you want to treat this [sickness], if the patient feels that pain, then do not appear before him as we have said, but grasp upon it gently and leave it alone and dry over that place until that cooped-up windiness has been discharged, and cauterize the place."
"But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat it by evacuating the body and using whatever expels windiness, and apply pills made of foul-smelling substances and pills of saffron and similar things."
"Regarding the extraction of arrows."
Arrows are diverse according to their species and the parts of the body where they hit, for their diversity is such that those with large points, small ones, concave ones, and blunt ones are solid; and there are those with three corners and those with tongues; also those with stars.
When an arrow strikes the head and passes through the bone, it can cause symptoms such as dizziness, headache, ringing in the ears, vertigo, redness of the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasm and confusion of understanding, vomiting bile, difficulty speaking or loss of voice, and there may be white, powdery moisture present at the wound site, with the surrounding skin appearing wet.
Therefore, if these signs appear, then you should abstain from treating and removing the arrow unless it is externally visible.
When an arrow strikes the heart near the left nipple and is embedded deeply in hard tissue, it may cause symptoms such as irregular heartbeat like the pulse, black blood flowing from the wound, coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, fainting, and sudden death. Therefore, you should know that death will occur immediately unless the patient receives prompt treatment.
When an arrow strikes the lung, there may be frothy blood sputum coming from the wound, and the vessels near the collarbone may become congested. The patient's complexion may change, they may exhale loudly or gasp for breath, and experience difficulty breathing due to pain.
If an arrow strikes the diaphragm (the veil of the ribs), there will be pain near the lower ribs. The patient may experience sudden, intense pain in the chest and back, accompanied by sighing and shortness of breath. All the muscles of the body may become rigid and tense.
If an arrow strikes the stomach, there will be vomiting of undigested food from the wound.
If an arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes lodged within it, there will be a visible wound or tear in the skin, tissue, or intestine, which may be ruptured. There is little hope for recovery if the arrow is removed in such cases.
If an arrow strikes the bladder and urine is excreted from the wound externally, with other bodily fluids flowing out as well, there will be severe pain in that area. You should know that such a wound is fatal.
"However, other members such as the face, neck, and back, shoulders and arms, spinal column, collarbone, thighs, legs, and their similar parts are saved according to major dispositions when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and is not venomous."
"I will tell you what I have seen about this kind of arrows, so that you may understand about their treatment."
"The reason for this is that when an arrow entered the tear duct of a man's eye near his nose, and you removed it from another opening, leaving behind the point of the arrowhead in his ear, he was healed and there was no harm done to his eye."
"And another arrow was removed from a Jew who had it in his eye near the eyelid, leaving behind the eyelid afterwards. The arrowhead was already covered and could not be found except for a small extremity that adhered to its shaft, which was a large, sharp Turkish arrow with a lion's head made of iron without ears."
"And the Jew recovered, and there was no harm done to his eye."
"And another arrow was removed from a Christian man who had it in his buttock. It was an Arab arrow with ears and you cut into it between two guide rings where it was already submerged in his buttock; then you pulled on it carefully and finally extracted it. The Christian man recovered."
"And another arrow was removed from a man who had it in his belly, and he thought that he would die because of it. Therefore, since thirty days had passed and there was no change in his condition, you cut into the arrow and extracted it, and he recovered, and there was no harm done to him."
"And I saw another man in whom an arrow had become stuck in his back, and the wound had closed over it. Since it had been there for about thirty years, the arrow was extracted from the root of the matter."
"And I saw a woman in whom an arrow had become stuck in her belly and the wound had closed over it. Her condition did not change in any way, nor was there any harm done to any of her natural functions."
"I saw another man in whom an arrow had become stuck in his face and the wound had healed over it. When the arrow was removed, he felt almost no pain."
"And the arrow of the king's steward that was stuck in his middle nose and had already deviated slightly to the right, was completely hidden."
"And you were called to treat him because of the entry wound of the arrow within three days."
"And therefore you opened the wound of the arrow very carefully. Then you inquired about it with a delicate touch and did not feel it, but he felt a puncture behind his right ear; therefore you thought that that puncture was because of the extremity of the arrow."
"And you widened the area with an ointment that has attractive and digestive virtue, making sure that the area around it is dry so that the location of the arrow becomes apparent."
"And there was no significant harm in the place where the arrow had reached it. And I continued to apply the ointment to him for many days, and there was no harm done. And the wound healed during that time, and he recovered from the pain of the extraction after several days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me so."
"And then you applied a corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it opened. And with your touch you felt that one end of the arrow adhered to its shaft."
"And approximately you adjusted the application of that corrosive medicine until I could see the extremity of the arrow very clearly. And I spent some time like this for about four months."
"And after the wound had become larger and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, I remained there and did not cease to devise and experiment with various methods until one day I began to extract it using various methods of traction with forceps made specifically for this purpose, as will be explained in detail later, until I finally extracted it; and then I restored that wound."
"And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound healed completely, and the pain was entirely cured without any residue remaining."
"And I tell you about the extraction of some arrows because you can pause here and find meaning in what I have not said, since the parts of this art and its definitions are not understood through mere narration nor comprehended by this book."
"And the wise artisan compares little things with many; and what is present with what is absent. And he finds new operations and new instruments whenever foreign causes occur."
"And therefore I say that arrows are not forced out of the limbs in which they have been stuck except by two methods: either by attraction of the place where it has entered, or by the opposite direction of the other part."
"And that which is forced out by the place where it entered is either an arrow proceeding from a fleshy location and is attracted and pushed out."
"And if it does not obey extraction at the time when it is caught, then you must leave it for some days until the flesh around it has softened and its attraction and exit are facilitated."
"And similarly if it is stuck in bone and does not obey extraction, then leave it for some days and repeat the attraction and movement each day until it comes out."
"And if it does not obey extraction even after some days have passed, then you must drill around the arrow in that bone with a fine awl until the location of the arrow is widened and then extract it."
"But if the arrow has already disappeared in the location of the body and is beyond sensation, then you should search for it with your touch. And if you feel it there, then attract it using some instruments which are suitable for that attraction."
"And if you cannot reach it because of the contraction of the wound and the length of the arrow, and here there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein, then cut through the wound until it is widened and the arrow can be extracted."
"And if it is stuck in the ear with which it is held, then carefully cut away all the flesh around where it has entered using any device you can devise. And devise means to facilitate the exit of the flesh through the fracture of the ears and torsion of those until it comes out."
When you administer the extraction of the arrow in any place where it may be, then use a tweezers with your hand and forceps at all parts within that it may perform that operation; and subtly with extreme care that the arrow is not broken and that it is made to your difficult attraction of it and that extraction.
And if you cannot extract the arrow during your time of work, then leave it for a few days until the parts that are in the circuit around it have healed, and then operate when the swelling has decreased. But if there is a flow of blood during your operation, then administer that which we have said about cure in its chapter.
And guard your virtue of incision of vein or nerve, or tendon, and administer ingeniously in every possible manner the evacuation of the arrow; and do this with ease and calmness and slowness and steadfastness as you and it is recommended.
And then cause the arrow to contract so that the wound is according to the figure according to which the arrow was designed to enter it, for it is more useful. But if this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you according to the figures.
But arrows that are shot in contrary of the other wound, or those from which some other wound proceeds to the wounds outside, or cause swelling around the end of the arrow by feeling the wound above the nearby bone and raise that prominence; then cut over it; and let the section be according to the quantity in which the forceps take hold of it; and then pull it out, for it is easily extracted.
But if it is retained in the bone, then twist your saw according to the contrary direction in which the arrow enters the bone, and amplify at its middle part, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it there for some days, and then extract it when it is loose enough to be pulled out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is in that, then push it through with it.
But if the shaft is broken and you want to administer impulse, then insert this, if it is blunt, turn concave so that its concavity enters the cavity of the arrow and then push it through with it.
But if the arrow is concave, then push this with force entering into that, for the arrow is relieved by this.
And if the arrow is barbed, then it is necessary for you to cut away all the flesh in which the wound is, if that is possible for you, and then treat it with whatever is appropriate.
And if the arrow enters the bladder, or the belly, or the thigh, or the side, and is close so that you can touch it with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut over it, then cut over it and guard yourself from cutting any vein or nerve, and extract it, and then stitch it up if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
Form of forceps with which arrows are extracted:
"Their extremities are like bird's beaks that are made with saw when they seize upon the arrow so that they do not let it go."
"Among these are made many species, large, few, and medium-sized, according to the quantity of the arrow's size and its thickness, and the width of the opening, and the tightness of it."
"Like this way so that it may ease the entrance of it into the concave arrow and is pushed with that."
"Venous phlebotomy."
"The veins that are usually phlebotomized are 30, of which 16 are in the head. These include two pulsating veins near the ears, called 'earlids', two veins visible on the temples, two veins located in the lacrimal canals of the eyes called 'tear ducts', one vein prepared at the middle of the forehead, and one vein located at the tip of the nose. There are also two veins on the neck and two veins on each lip. Additionally, there are two veins located behind the tongue." "The veins that are phlebotomized in the arms numbered five. These include one cephalic vein located on the outer side of the arm which is commonly called 'the vein of the head'. There is also a median vein formed by branches of the basilica and cephalic veins, known as 'the vein of the whole body' to the common people. The basilica vein is located on the inner side of the arm and is called 'citillar' or 'vena del ventre' in vulgar language. The radial vein lies over the forearm and is used for phlebotomy when it appears prominently at the pulse. The savatella vein lies between the auricular nerve and the little finger, consisting of two branches."
"And in the thigh and leg there are three veins: one is located on the outer side of the curvature of the knee joint on the outer thigh, another is the saphenous vein located on the inner side of the thigh near the knee joint, and the third is the sciatic vein located on the outer side of the thigh. There are also other veins in the leg similar to these."
"The benefit of phlebotomizing the veins near the ears is for treating ancient headaches, migraines, facial tics and ulcers on the face or head."
"And the method of phlebotomizing those veins is as follows: first, shave the patient's head. Then, rub the head with oil on both sides of the occiput using a large cloth and good friction. Next, make the patient bend their neck with a cloth tied around it so that two points near the ears appear at the front of the head. Use your index finger to find those veins and feel their pulse."
"Then take the lancet called 'cutellar', which is pointed, and introduce it into the vein on the elbow until blood comes out." "And raise your hand with the vein and elbow upwards, then make an incising cut into the vein with a pointed incision." "And make the length of the incision about two fingers' width or thereabouts, then let as much blood flow out as you wish."
"And afterwards, apply powder and a bandage to it so that it may heal."
"Before the start of this book, there was an incision made on them and their cauterization."
"More often than not, the two venous arteries that surround the temples serve as the location for phlebotomy in ancient times to treat migraine, headache, and frequent eye disorders. They also help to alleviate excess heat from the body."
"And the quality of performing phlebotomy on them is according to what you recommend to him: constraint the patient's illness with your hand firmly and visibly appearing on both veins, demonstrating the pulse of their vessels beneath your fingers. And then you will know how to proceed."
"And thus you raise the cord above the vein at its branches using your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet beneath it, raising the vein up. Then cut it similarly as you did with the other two veins, allowing blood to flow according to your need."
"And thus release the pressure of the illness, and keep your finger on the vein for about an hour. Then apply a compress to that area with cotton and powder, strengthening it above with firm constraint, allowing it to heal within."
"And already at the beginning of this book, the memory of them (the veins) and their incision have been preceded."
"And therefore, phlebotomy of the frontal vein is appropriate for treating ancient facial diseases such as cephalic migraine and rigor (spasm), and also for fetid ulcers."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on him (the patient) is according to what I recommend to you: so that the illness strengthens its own vein with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument called 'fossor' whose form is as follows:"
"Press the prominent point that is at the head of the 'fossor' upon that vein and percuss it with your thumb or another cause in this manner, allowing blood to flow according to the quantity you desire. Then release the perforation of the illness and strengthen it within so that it may heal."
"And sometimes it is useful to use a blunt phlebotomy lancet, but it is not necessary that the phlebotomy lancet have an acute extremity like the others. Rather, its lateral extremity should be blunted so that the bone does not break it when it is thin."
"But the recommendation of these two veins (the angular and frontal veins) is for the lacrimal glands of both eyes for scabies of the eye with rigor and seborrhoea in the eyes, and for facial diseases."
"And the quality of phlebotomy is that you strengthen his vein with a bandage, and then perform phlebotomy on him, standing above his head."
"And let the phlebotomy be done obliquely along its length with a small phlebotomy lancet, because in that place there is not much flesh. And if the phlebotomy lancet is thin, it may break its point."
"And then let blood flow as much as you need, and pause on that spot, and strengthen it overnight with a bandage, and then release it."
"But phlebotomy of the nasal vein is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and also for facial diseases, migraine, rigor that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on him is that you strengthen his vein with your left hand and then take hold of his nose with your right hand. Then take the sharp phlebotomy lancet and place it in the middle of the head of the nose according to rectitude, because the vein does not appear to touch here, and because blood begins to flow immediately."
"It is necessary that you withdraw your hand gradually with the phlebotomy lancet and make blood flow according to your need. Then bind it overnight because that vein heals easily."
"But the benefit of phlebotomy on both saphenous veins is for constriction of the knee and for the beginning of leprosy, and for many diseases, itching, and corrupt ulcers like herpes zoster."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on those veins is that the patient should strengthen each vein with a bandage around his thigh. Then the operator should stand above the patient's head while the patient is seated on a chair."
"And then perform phlebotomy on the long saphenous vein with an ample phlebotomy lancet little by little. Then extract blood according to a moderate quantity or according to what seems necessary at that time. Then do the same for the other vein."
"And then untie the bandage and strengthen both veins with a middle ligature so that the patient is not fainted. Then leave him until morning because the swelling will be healed."
"And the benefit of phlebotomy on the veins of the arm is for small abscesses and pustules of the mouth, corruption of the gums, and corrupt ulcers and fissures. Also for ulcers that are in the nose and around it."
"And the quality of phlebotomy on those veins is that you make the patient sit before you and strengthen his vein with a bandage. Then turn back his lips and observe both veins, one of which is on the right side of the lip and the other on the left. The demonstration of each is by its blackness, and especially because there are small black veins around them. Then cut each one with an incising incision."
"But if there is doubt about which vein you should cut, then observe the incision of the larger one and the manifest pus from that one."
"And perform phlebotomy on both veins that are in the upper lips."
"Of the two veins that are on either side of the tongue, it is most appropriate for phlebotomy to be done at the level of the cephalic branches that are in the gulley, at the malady of the uvula, and at maladies of the mouth."
"And the quality of phlebotomizing this is that one follows the malady between their two largest veins, elevates their tongue, and regards both sides of it, observing the vein on one side and the vein on the other; and the color of each is inclining towards blackness."
"And then phlebotomize each one; but be careful not to pierce too deeply with your hand in phlebotomizing these ones, for there are arteries nearby."
"And the veins that are phlebotomized in the elbow are those which are commonly found in all men, and their phlebotomy is performed in two ways: either by putting a mirtilated or olivated flebotomy needle to subtly incline, or by cutting with a flebotomy scalpel, and calling it if necessary; and this is its form:"
"It should be like this: first, as often as possible, and adapt to opening the veins that are concave and full, proceeding with apparent large ones; and those that contain thick, turbid blood."
"And this phlebotomy has a smaller width and more subtle tip; it is suitable for phlebotomizing those veins that contain subtle, citrine-colored blood."
"But the main benefit of phlebotomy from the basilica vein (one of three veins) is that it extracts blood from those illnesses which are on the left side of the neck and collar, and from whatever affects the chest and belly."
"And it is appropriate for the phlebotomist, when performing a phlebotomy on that [vein], to carefully watch over it and be cautious in it, because there is an artery nearby; if he errs in the puncture of the phlebotomy needle, he will injure that artery and cause a flow of blood. Therefore, it should not be phlebotomized with a pointed phlebotomy needle, but rather cutting with a scalpel."
"And if the basilica is not apparent by manifest appearance, then you should leave it and move to another one of its branches; or phlebotomize at the end of the arm where that is manifest. And cut that with a scalpel needle as we have said."
"But if you want to phlebotomize the median vein, then first at the bend of the arm where you know the pulse location, and approach gently with your fingers. Then lift up the arm and cut the vein with a concave section using a scalpel needle as we have said; and be careful not to cut too deeply for some space from the artery."
"And when you press with strength, if it swells in the place where you have touched with your fingers, then that swelling is a swelling of the artery, and therefore avoid that."
"And if the blood flows in the phlebotomy like a child's urine, and the blood is red, then know that it is blood from the artery. Then quickly lift your finger off of it for some distance; remove your finger, and if the blood ceases, because often it does cease, then press on the arm and leave it."
"And guard against the disease of the wound and be careful with it for some days until it is healed so that it does not fester."
"But if the blood does not cease and you know that no medicine is present for this, then you have injured the artery. If it appears to you, because its extremity is contracted and the blood has ceased; or take a cloth of figs and press upon it, and then take one handbreadth of lint and press it on the place of the vein with pressure descending with a bandage and powder for another day."
"And then if the blood has ceased, that is good. But if not, then cure it with whatever was mentioned earlier at the recollection of the place where the powders cease the flow of blood."
"And the stopping of the blood from it is difficult in many dispositions due to the small size of the vein and the possibility of injury to the arm."
"But phlebotomy of the dark vein is useful because it removes blood from the rising part of the head and from the descending part of the trunk. For this reason, because it is composed of branches of the basilica and branches of the cephalica, as we have said."
"But it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be careful in phlebotomizing that one because nearby there is a nerve. And therefore if he errs in the puncture of the phlebotomy needle and touches the nerve, then stupor will occur, and its healing is difficult; often it does not heal completely. This nerve can appear to sensation; but sometimes it may be hidden at some people and is subtle and not visible. Then you should pause the phlebotomy with which you phlebotomize with a scalpel section, and avoid the nerve with your strength."
"And if the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise."
"The benefit of phlebotomizing the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and alleviates diseases of the eyes."
"And in this particularly, if you want the phlebotomist to puncture with an olive-shaped needle or a myrtle-shaped needle; because it is safer than all other veins, for there are neither artery nor nerve nearby."
"But often an aposteme appears in some people when they are not phlebotomized at the first percussion; but do not touch that aposteme."
"The first one is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the conservation of health and with hope of improvement and caution against the occurrence of diseases. And it should be done on one of the veins which are in the elbow: cephalic, dark, or basilica; and it should be done at the beginning of spring, fasting in spring and autumn, and after eating in winter when signs of repletion appear."
"And phlebotomy should be performed on the third day or at least three hours later than midday."
"But phlebotomy that is administered when convenience and necessity require it can be done at any time, day or night, at any hour and at any time."
"And children should not be phlebotomized until they are fourteen years old; and pregnant women should not be phlebotomized except in the fourth month and up to seven months. And elderly people who have already passed sixty years should not be phlebotomized."
"And when someone wants to be phlebotomized for any reason, they should cleanse their bowels beforehand with a lion's claw enema; especially if there is much retained feces in them. For if the veins absorb superfluities from the intestines during phlebotomy, which have been cleansed, it will not harm the principal members."
"Let not patients suffering from sacred and profane drunkenness nor those who flee from sickness or drunkenness be phlebotomized until this is removed from them; also let not patients with dropsy be phlebotomized. Rather, phlebotomy should be feared in cases of sudden passion, vomiting, windiness of the belly, multiplication of coitus and labor, excessive exercise during fasts or vigils, or any cause that dissolves virtue."
"And consider in the refinement of humors beforehand whether the blood is thick, from excessive foods and drinks, and if so, medicinally thin it if possible."
"And also enter into a bath, if no cause forbids it, or exercise somewhat for the blood to be subtilized."
"And pause the phlebotomy from there in the foot until about the hour of Terce, as we have said."
"And continue so that the foot of him who has been phlebotomized is guarded from all evil animal accidents, such as anxiety, anger, fear, and from all corporal accidents, such as excessive labor, pain, coitus, and like them."
"And let his seat be prepared with the causes in use by men with aromaticity and fragrance, and with balsams and like them according to their quantity of possibility."
"And follow the phlebotomist on a cushion that is softer than the cushion of him who is to be phlebotomized."
"And then extend his arm, and rub that of the phlebotomist with his own hand two or three times; and then strengthen the ligature with a rope and wrap it twice."
"And let the ligature be moderate, for if it is not equal, or there is superfluity in the ligature, then fluxes of blood are prohibited; or if it is with softening, then the course of the blood is impeded equally."
"And then, before making the ligature, let him who is to be phlebotomized rub his hands together until the veins are inflated and clearly felt."
"And then the phlebotomist should anoint his lancet with a little of proper old oil; and then place his index finger of his left hand over that vein where he wishes to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein may flee a bit, and percuss the vein gently until one finds a vein that feels like a cord fleeing under the phlebotomy site. And those veins are full of windiness, and when one pauses the lancet on them, they contract under the lancet, and become visible to the phlebotomist; and if the lancet touches the vein, then it is narrow."
"And thus, let the phlebotomist be careful and attentive to all these causes."
"And then pause the lancet, but if the vein is not opened in one attempt, then it is good. And if not, repeat that attempt on another vein nearby, or press down with haste, if there is no aposteme."
"And if the site is apostemated or the patient becomes worse, then let him be left for a day or two, and do not strengthen the ligature, lest it draw out the aposteme too violently, nor introduce into baths."
"And then repeat the phlebotomy if necessary."
"And if the opening is small and blood flows subtly, and you fear that not enough blood will flow out, then repeat the lancet in that tract according to rectitude and adjust it a little at the opening, and do this with haste before the site becomes apostemated, for the site becomes apostemated in most men with but little opening."
"But if the site is already apostemated, then do not repeat upon it, lest you have cause for blame. And pause upon some part of the oil groove, for it alleviates the course of the blood." "And it is better in this location that oil, and other oils."
"And similarly administer an oil groove in all phlebotomies where veins are opened against the prohibition of blood flow, and do this with treacle, almonds, and saffron when pausing from one of these upon the site, for blood is subtilized and circulated when it is thick."
"And if a large aposteme is generated at the site of phlebotomy, which often happens, and especially to those who are not bled until that attempt, or where the vein opens but little, then apply a poultice, and pause upon the site with a sponge infused in water and salt, a little scraped, and press it for an hour, for it dissolves."
"And he should do this also at the time of stopping the flow of blood from that vein with a ligature or another vein."
"And if there remains in the site for some days a little blackness or greenness, do not notice it. And if you wish, pause upon that with some cause of aloe and myrrh dissolved, or some cause of mint juice or chamomile, or similar."
"And many times apostemes and elevations occur due to phlebotomy at the basilica [i.e., temple of the head]."
"And then pause your hand upon that, and if you find softness under your compression on it, then that elevation is evil and bad. And therefore be careful to pause upon it with some cause of what we have said, lest blood flow violently from the artery."
"And make an emplaster with that in which there is astringency, so that the site may be endured. And then treat it with another cure until it is healed."
"And each person should have blood extracted from him according to the quantity of his virtue and according to the color prevailing over his blood as it appears."
"For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it becomes red. And similarly if you find it thick, then let it flow out until it becomes subtilized. And likewise if it is acute, until its sharpness is abated."
"And he should amplify the phlebotomy of the vein and make the lancet broad for that person who is full and strong and has need of bloodletting in one attempt."
"And in the thin person, let the opposite be done, and let that person have blood extracted from him in several attempts, and let the tract be narrow."
"And the best that is administered in phlebotomy of veins is a transverse section, not puncture."
"And this manner of phlebotomy is saved from flux of blood and incision of nerves. And it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy according to latitude and longitude."
"And to that one upon whom syncope is accustomed to occur during phlebotomy, you should take care of him before phlebotomy with a little bread infused in verjuice or simple syrup scraped. And extract blood from him in three or four attempts."
"And if there is coldness of temperament, then take before phlebotomy bread infused in syrup of myrrh or in aromatic syrup sweetened with spices such as pepper, cinnamon, ginger and the like, or in syrup that is aromatic and very fragrant."
"And if syncope occurs during phlebotomy and it is due to much blood loss, then let him drink water of flesh and very fragrant syrup. And use aromatics with aloe, and let him rub his forehead with that."
"And use the other causes that we have mentioned in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation."
"To swell the patient's arm and dissolve their blood according to their constitution, apply to them that which is suitable for much evacuation, whose power is frog, which expels blood gradually, as per the quantity of its power in continuous days."
"He who wants to swell his arm and draw his blood again, and whose body is robust, let him do this about seven or nine hours after the first phlebotomy."
"He who wants to draw blood from his body against the part that declines, let it be done on the second or third day after swelling."
"He who has little blood already shed in his body and feels weak and experiences fever, let a lot of blood be drawn from him at once, and increase the aperture gradually until he faints; but you must keep all the conditions of phlebotomy; and place your hand on his pulse while letting the blood out, so that death does not occur instead of fainting, for many times this happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent."
"And when you want to release the arm and evacuate the blood again, after the opening of the vein has already closed and it is difficult to draw blood, do not press hard under that arm with a curved hand, for that is too rough."
"After performing phlebotomy again, let him rest until he is phlebotomized a second time; or remove the acuteness of the phlebotomy that has coagulated blood in the opening of the vein; or place upon it some tyriaca alfarach, or sagzeneia, and compress it with light compression until the blood is completely expelled."
"And if the vein is inflamed, leave that one alone and do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided."
"And if there is a need to evacuate blood again and there is no excuse, then do it on the same spot or in another vein of the other arm."
"And the vein called 'funis of the arm' is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic or basilic veins when they are not visible or are hidden, for it consists of those."
"And the quality of performing phlebotomy on him is that the patient immerses his hand in cold water until the vein becomes engorged and appears clearly."
"And approach should be made with a bandage with moderate tension over that with a median strap; and phlebotomy should be performed according to the transverse line, not latitudinally nor longitudinally."
"And let the opening be wide, and perform your phlebotomy with the same vein that you phlebotomize above the junction of the hand a little."
"And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse your hand in cold water again and let the blood run into the water until your need is satisfied."
"But if you are in the days of summer, then you are excused from immersing your hand in cold water, and in winter, it is prohibited to cool the body with cold water."
"And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than that of all other veins, for it does not have a pulsating vein or nerves."
"And phlebotomy of the saphenous vein on the right hand is beneficial for diseases of the liver."
"And the quality of performing phlebotomy on him is that you bind the hand with a bandage or your hand, after having placed it in cold water, until the vein becomes inflamed and clearly visible to view; and approach and phlebotomize it transversely; and if you hit upon something else, do not remove that from there."
"And be careful not to finish the phlebotomy with your hand, for beneath it are the nerves of those denuded of flesh."
"And approach again immerses the hand in cold water and let the blood run into that, for if you do not immerse it again in the cold water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and it is prohibited to flow."
"And when blood has been extracted according to the quantity needed, then place upon the vein oil and salt so that it does not become consolidated suddenly; and do likewise in all veins that are straight."
"But the benefit of phlebotomy on this vein of the left hand is that it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen."
"And do likewise in performing phlebotomy on him as you have done in the other."
"And the benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for diseases that are in the lower parts of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menses, and for diseases of the thighs and ulcers of old sores, and similar diseases."
"And the quality of performing phlebotomy on him is that the patient immerses his foot in cold water and makes friction until the veins appear clearly; and he places a bandage over the junction of the foot; and the point of the vein appears below the said projection, and from there it branches into many small ramifications on the surface of the foot."
"And then phlebotomize the most prominent branch or that which is below the heel below the aggregation of it, for it is better and safer."
"And if you phlebotomize it on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that are beneath it on the surface of the foot, and finish your phlebotomy with the one with which you phlebotomize transversely, as if you wanted to incise it."
"And let the phlebotomy be sufficient."
"But if it is prohibited to let blood, then immerse his foot again in cold water and let the blood run into that until it is evacuated."
"And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then approach again with the phlebotomy tool and remove a little more, for the location is safe; and do not fear hurting him, but be careful of the nerves as we have said."

"And of the sciatic vein, its location is as we have said beneath the heel below the head of the fibula on the outer side."

"And then when it appears, then phlebotomize it according to each vein that you can and according to the transverse direction, which is better; or incise it with incision or cut it with sectioning, for its location is safe."

"Of cupping (ventosization) and of the qualities of using those instruments."
"Cupping is done with horns, or earthenware, or glass."
"And cupping is done in two ways: one with scarification and bloodletting, the other without scarification."
"And this wound-cleansing is done in two ways, either by fire or without fire."
"And the thirteen locations on the body where wounds are administered with scarification and bloodletting are as follows: the first is placed on the forehead, which is the final part of the head; the middle one between the two parts of the neck; those under the chin near the jawbone but below the mouth; those on both spatulas; those on either shoulder, located at the end of the upper arm; those on each elbow, which are at the bend of the arm; and finally, those on both thighs."
"And bloodletting does not extract blood from the large veins, but only from the small ones scattered throughout the flesh. Therefore, it does not draw out the humor in that way, just as phlebotomy does; nor is it necessary to perform bloodletting with fire or without fire in any disease caused by excess, until the entire body has been purged."
"But if bloodletting is necessary due to illness or continuity, it should be administered at the beginning, middle, and end of each month, as well as at any other appropriate time."
"And thus, whenever any of these causes occur, and especially if it is on the third or middle day of the month, we command bloodletting to be performed after two or three days have passed from that day."
"And the benefit of bloodletting from the neck is that it helps to alleviate heaviness in the head and prevent fainting spells. However, it is necessary that this be an appropriate evacuation of the entire body."
"And this bloodletting takes place at the location for phlebotomy on the cephalic vein."
"It is not recommended to perform bloodletting on someone with plethoric constitution or a catarrhal condition, as this can cause great harm."
"And therefore, it is not necessary to perform bloodletting on the elderly, nor on those whose illnesses are caused by heat."
"But whoever persists in bloodletting will forget this practice. Therefore, it is necessary that you command the phlebotomist to pause his hand slightly before removing the cupping vessel at the end of the session, so as to prevent the generation of forgetfulness."
"But bloodletting for asthmatics takes place at the locations for phlebotomy of the dark vein and basilica vein. For this reason, it helps to relieve asthma, constriction of the throat, splitting of the windpipe, coughs, and congestion."
"And it is necessary to withdraw the cupping vessel for asthmatics slightly, because if it is paused too long, it can generate weakness in the body and stomach."
"And bloodletting for headaches helps to relieve accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia, migraine, and inflammation, as well as pains in the roots of teeth. It takes place at the location for phlebotomy of the basilica vein."
"And it is necessary that you command the phlebotomist not to press his hand during scarification, so that he does not pierce the artery and cause bleeding."
"And bloodletting from the chin helps to relieve bad breath in the mouth, gum disease, and other oral conditions. It takes place at the location for phlebotomy of the artery that is on both lips."
"But bloodletting performed between the two spatulas helps to relieve heatstroke, which occurs due to an excess of heat."
"And bloodletting performed between the two divisions of the cheeks helps to relieve flebotomy of three veins: the basilica, dark, and cephalic veins. This is because it attracts blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh, as well as from the other small veins connected to the larger veins, so that the attraction reaches these three veins."
"And command the phlebotomist not to proceed with scarification because that location is devoid of flesh and therefore has nerves and arteries close beneath the surface."
"And it is bloodletting performed on the shanks that helps to relieve hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcers of the perineum."
"And it is necessary that you command the phlebotomist to perform a large bloodletting with a sharp cup because this location requires strong extraction, and subtle cups do not draw much blood when used alone; therefore it should be scarified."
"But bloodletting performed on the fleshy part of the forearm helps to relieve chronic pains in the kidneys and womb, as well as the bladder. It stimulates menstruation and relieves both dropsy and obesity. It takes place at the location for phlebotomy of two saphenous veins, and the horizontal vein, as well as two small veins."
"But it can also greatly weaken the entire body and cause fainting in many men."
"And bloodletting performed on the horizontal vein offers relief similar to that of bloodletting performed on both arms."
"The quality of the position of bloodletting is that at the beginning, the cup should be placed vertically and followed by gentle suction, without prolonging the position of bloodletting too much. Instead, pause it lightly and remove it carefully so that the humors come to the location naturally." "And do not cease repeating this until you see that the location has become reddened and swollen, and that the blood begins to appear."
"And then scarify and repeat suction little by little, taking care to consider the disposition of the patient's constitution."
"For that person who has tender skin with many pores, it is necessary for you to scarify him only once and not more than that, so that the location does not become ulcerated."
"And command the phlebotomist to widen the scarification slightly and press down a little, while gently applying suction."
"If the blood is thick and coarse, then it is necessary to scarify twice. For in the first scarification, the path for the subtle exit of blood and water is opened." "But in the second one, the extraction of thick blood is acquired through scrutiny."
"If the blood is very fetid, then it is necessary to perform a third scarification in order to achieve the desired result."
"Finally, when we want to extract blood little by little or just a small amount, one scarification suffices."
"And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we should perform many scarifications. But if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to perform scarifications with a deep cut."
"The term 'scarification' refers only to the depth of the wound made by the lancet."
"And if the person has thick, hard, dark skin with narrow pores, then it is necessary to anoint the locations of bloodletting with gentle, opening, resolvent oils. If it is summer time, use oils such as aloe oil, violet oil, or double-dosed melilot oil, or cumin seed oil. But if it is winter time, then use narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood oil for those of a similar nature."
"If excess fluid is thick, then use oil of marjoram, or oil of balm, or oil of aloe, or oil of nettle, and their like."
"If the wound is of large pores with tender flesh, then oil should be prohibited, and the places near the wound should have their bandages washed with warm water or hot water of salt, or cold water of sage, or cold water of borago, etc., according to those."
"And for one whose blood has great humidity, wash their wounds with sour wine, or water of myrtle and sumac, etc., like those."
"And for one whose fluids are thick, wash their wounds with old wine, or water of hyssop (marjoram), or decoction of fennel, or chamomile, etc., like those."
"And it is necessary that the bandage be kept before washing; having to be administered near the bath for one or two hours, and having to sleep near the wound."
"What is necessary for regulating the bandage of a patient who has been bled before washing."
"At the beginning, if the patient or one who needs phlebotomy is choleric and vents over his hot blood and inflammation, then he should take causes of cooling, such as sour apples, endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secanabin, and julep, etc., like those."
"And pause in their foods such as poles, fowl meat, cooked foods with sour sauces, etc., like those."
"And for one whose complexion is phlegmatic, it is necessary that he drink hyssop syrup with honey, or hyssop syrup of sesame seeds and sumac, and a middle-aged wine between old and new; and that he be advised to use moderation in drinking and eating, and avoid foods such as poultry, rabbit, partridge, and pigeon."
"And it is necessary that on the day of washing and phlebotomy, drinking be greater than eating."
"And it is necessary to give some people triaca syrup or musk medicine (musk), or alcelita before washing or phlebotomy or during, so that their main members are comforted and their blood is subtilized; but those with a scrofula should not take it."
"But the washings that are not scars are those paused on the liver, and on the spleen and podagra, and on the navel, in the locations of the joints, and the armpit of the arm, for these members do not suffer scarification upon them."
"And it is not desired for those except the attraction of blood from a member, such as pausing a wash on the nipple in illness of bleeding of blood from the nose; and they use it so that cold and thickened humors of the member may be dissolved, such as pausing on the belly and navel; for it rarifies the member, scalds it, and relieves pain because it resolves humors."
"And it is paused on the temples when accidents occur in their oppression or apoplexy, for by virtue of strong attraction it draws away oppression and expels apoplexy from its place."
"And similarly, it does when paused on the liver and spleen to resolve humors enclosed therein."
"And these washings are administered solely with suction; some times they are administered with fire."
"And sometimes they are administered filled with warm water in illnesses of the spleen."
"And this is so that the washing may be full and larger, it should be cold water alone or water in which convenient herbs have been cooked."
"Form of washing administered with fire: Let the circumference of its mouth be that of two fingers, as we have formed it; and let the quantity of coals be the most of one handful; and let them be on the side of it, in the place that is in the circle, the most of it, and let the traction be small according to the quantity that enters it like a needle."
"Let them be made of red hot coals or citrus, with thick edges, lion's teeth, and smooth for that they not wound the member because of its position upon it."
"And let there be in its midst a transverse tongue of iron or of brass, on which is placed a candle with fire."
"And let this washing be much larger than that which we have recommended, and the smaller one according to the illnesses and state of he who uses it."
"And washings for children and weak ones are different from those for young people and strong ones."
"And the quality of pausing this washing with fire on a member is that it extends the hand with fire from decently dried wood before a small candle of wax, and pauses it on the midst of the transverse tongue in the middle place of the washing, so that the fire be kindled around the edge thereof so that not burn the body of the sick person."
"And then it is paused on the member of the sick person, and let the thread be upon the traction that we have said, until when we have the washings and do not desire to stretch the thread and that smoke issue forth upon that traction, then the washing will soon dissolve."
"And then remove the hand according to its manner, and repeat it if necessary."
"But in washing that is administered for the illness of pleurisy with water, there being neither transverse tongue nor traction, and it is not administered until it is filled with water, and is paused on the member alone."
"And this is its form: And this washing, the larger it is, the better, for that it can receive a large quantity of water."

"Bloodlettings are not administered on most members except those where it is impossible to apply washings because of their smallness, such as the eye and gum and like them; or on member denuded of flesh, such as the finger and nose and their like."
"And the quality of using those bloodlettings is if found sanguissuguas that are in the waters of ditches free from putrefaction."
"And then they are laid the day and night in water ditch until they be hungry, and that there remain none in the belly of those, and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning by phlebotomy and washings, and then rub the member of the sick person until it becomes reddened; and then pause them on it."

"And if it is possible to suck the place with a washing, it is more expedient in healing."

"And it is necessary, if necessary, iteration of sanguissuguas when it is possible to change those."
