Here begins the second part: of restoration. This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine; it concerns the reduction and repositioning of fractured or dislocated bones.
But know, my son, that only fools and commoners boast about this chapter, those who have never regarded the books of the Ancients nor read even one letter from them.
And this is why this method is not practiced among the learned in our land. And yet I have not forgotten it during all these years of my life; and I have not included what follows except for the long study of the books of the Ancients and understanding of them, from which I extracted this science.
And now I direct you to experiments and studies during the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science contains and on which I have experimented, since I have summarized it for you here and freed you from the occupation of lengthening it. And I have summarized it at the end of summarizing; and I have demonstrated it at the end of explaining; and I have formed many shapes of instruments that are administered in it, as is done by addition to explanation, just as I have done in two preceding chapters.
Chapter one
Summary and collections of cases of bone fractures, from which a preliminary explanation is necessary before we begin the reminder of broken limbs and their varieties in each case. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of my speech; and the chapters for understanding which at the beginning it is necessary to force the artisans and to stay on the truth of them; that is you and those who are prompt that know or learn this noble art other than you.
And therefore I say that when there occurs fracture or dislocation or torsion or casing, it is necessary for you to begin immediately with phlebotomy on him or to let him bleed, or sometimes both, if there are no prohibitions such as debility of virtue, or he is an infant, or very old, or it is a time of intense heat or cold.
And it should be content with his usual diet of fresh herbs and meats of birds and crabs; and prohibit thick meats and filling foods and all foods that fill the veins with blood until you are certain of an abscess cut and if you do not fear effusion of matter in the limb.
And immediately return the sick to his usual first regimen with which he is accustomed to live.
And therefore when the bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary for the sick to be nourished with nutritious foods that are rich and heavy and viscous, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, rice, heads, feet, bellies of cows, oil, fresh cheese, thick wine and similar ones. And with this regimen coagulation of fracture occurs more easily and quickly.
And know that the bones of young people when they are in their first growth and old men do not have the ability to be united and consolidated according to their nature because of the dryness of their limbs.
But nevertheless nature is able and accustomed to unite over the broken bone of any part without any similar cause such as glutinousness that adheres and strengthens it until they are completely united and fixed together until the end of solidity and firmness, just like fire at the beginning, provided that no cause prohibits this from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary to pause the foods of the sick, foods that are heavy and viscous and thick, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from that of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from that of the arm; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.
And I come with remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in its own chapter separately from the others.
Great diversity even within itself. And the species of bone fractures are diversified within themselves; for there is an arch fracture which occurs in stars, and sometimes fractures occur according to the length of the bone: these are called spiral fractures and zigzag fractures and additional ones that do not proceed, such as those that occur when a pen is prepared to write. And there is a fracture with swelling and disruption at the head, and a small fissure fracture.
And for each species there is a specific pain or torment in its restoration according to what will come later in its own chapter in their places.
Many signs of bone fracture. And for this reason a bone fracture is recognized by its tortuosity, and the prominence and depression of it to the touch, and that when you grasp it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent tortuosity in the place and it is not done with your touch when you shake the bone and the sick does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
It is possible that there may be tortuosity or a small fissure, so you should not move it with your hand and squeeze it too much. Rather pause on it with the medications of which mention will be made at that moment which are appropriate for the place; and rather apply traction by subtle restriction.
And know that the place where it is arched and not split into two or other than spiral fractures, since all parts of its comparison are removed, then you should begin immediately with the correction of it provided that it suffers from cauterizing abscess.
And if it suffers from cauterizing abscess, then leave it alone for a few days until the abscess ceases. And afterwards move it in whatever way you can of elevation and exercise.
And know that the restoration and union of it is more easy than for the bone in which a spiral fracture occurs.
And therefore strengthen it according to what will come later about it.
But if the bone has spiral fractures, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from two parts if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two strings or with one string and your hand.
And be in such a position that you hold the member at the place where it is moved according to its natural figure while the parts of the broken member are extended, then persevere in returning those additions with exercise and movement according to what you can do in their places.
And guard yourself according to your ability that the sick does not suffer because of your operation of them or injury. And persevere according to your ability in applying one bone to its companion according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that you touch it at that opportune moment with your hand and if there is any different cause here, correct it and move it according to the quantity of your virtue.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as do many foolish doctors who often cause cauterizing abscess because of their operation or cause contraction in the member as we have seen from the operations of many.
"And the quality of the tightening of the broken limb is what I recommend. For you should know that broken limbs are diverse in their smallness and greatness, and in their form."
"And for those that are small, such as the arm, hand, and foot, and similar ones, then the wrapping should be made of very soft cloth."
"But those that are large, such as the thigh or calf, require a strong and firm wrapping, because the tight wrapping connects the member strongly on all sides with equal tightening, so that there is no crease in it."
"And begin thus: carefully start your wrapping of the limb conveniently over the site of that fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the member requires, and tighten your hand a little with the bandage."
"And then continue down to that limb beyond the highest point of the fracture site, and tighten it even more than you did with the wrapping with which you tightened the fracture site." "And then extend your wrapping beyond the fracture site somewhat, and gradually loosen the tightening until you reach a considerable distance from it."
"And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken site with wrapping, and continue down beyond the fracture site with wrappings; and your operation should be the same in the tightening of the wrapping and loosening of that according to what we have said concerning the first wrapping above."
"And then pause between the wrappings with a piece of wool or cloth with which to straighten out the crookedness of the fracture if there is any crookedness in it; and if not, do not pause in it for any reason."
"And then wrap another bandage over it, and also wrap stars decently made of pitch-plaster around these wrappings if there is no inflammation or cauterized ulcer in the site, and otherwise wrap around that which covers the ulcer or inflamed area; and leave it like that for a few days; and then tighten over those star plasters."
"And let the stars be made of soft white linen cloth, skillfully formed; or they may be made of pine wood; or branches of palm; and let each star be of this size and shape:"
"However, the plaster that is placed over that fracture should be thicker and wider than the other plasters, and its length should be according to what the limb is in size and smallness. And then tighten over the plasters with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then tie it above with thread skillfully as we have said concerning tightening, and let your tightening be according to the extent of the fracture site, and the further away from the fracture, the less the tightening."
"And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of wool; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish people doing when they use threads of twisted thread for tying, it is a great error, because the tightening will not be even."
"Also very fine threads are not convenient, because you will not be able to tighten with those what you want, and it is not necessary that there should be less than a finger's breadth between plaster and plaster."
"And if the patient feels pain in the extremities of the plasters near the tightening in those places, then remove them with soft wool or carded wool until it no longer hurts him."
"Sometimes fracture occurs with luxation, and dislocation occurs in the elbow, as I have mentioned in that chapter concerning singularities."
"And know that not every broken limb should be plastered on the first day; the reason for this is that when a limb is large, it does not require plasters to be placed upon it until about five or seven days have passed, according to your certainty of the advent of an inflamed ulcer."
"And this is the manner of plasters with which the Ancients used to treat fractures, luxations, and deformities."
"Manner of plaster commonly used for the treatment of fractures, convenient for many complexities and especially for women and children, because it does not cause heat or friction: take flour powder, which is the bran that remains after grinding grain in a mill."
"And sieve it thus as it is with albumin of egg white, and use it without making it too thick or fine."
"Another manner of plaster for treating fracture, luxation, and deformity: take asafetida, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mustard seed which is the residue of wild mustard, all of these in equal parts, five drachmas each; myrrh and aloe also five drachmas each; aloes wood twenty drachmas; all are ground and sieved and mixed with aloetic water or egg white if the patient's condition is scabbed. And use this plaster, because it has a good composition and restores lightly broken bones, beneficial for many people."
"Another manner of plaster which is beneficial for fracture and deformity: take mustard seed and white wax fifteen drachmas each, myrrh and aloe five drachmas each, acacia six drachmas, pitch twenty drachmas."
"All are ground in a mortar with much effort and sieved, and mixed with water or egg white and applied."
"Manner of plaster for joints and separated bones in their places, and when pain occurs accidentally in those and for fissures and fractures: take clean wool and submerge it in sour wine and cooked oil, and place it on the location."
"But in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, of inflamed ulcer, nor does it properly expel pains."
"Another manner of plaster that restores broken bone: take leaves from a fig tree and leaves from wild poppy, count them all together and make plaster of them."
"A brief manner of plaster administered for the restoration of broken bone and when you want resolution of the remnant of ulcer: take residues of white wax, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, barley flour, all in equal parts by weight; grind them all together and mix with wine if the limb is not scabbed."
"But if it is scabbed, then use it mixed with wet coriander water or plain water."
"Another manner of plaster more powerful in resolution than the first, when a hard ulcer occurs during bone restoration: take residues of white wax and its seed, and of balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile flowers, all in equal parts by weight; grind them together and mix with willow water or sweet water or wine, all according to the quality of the limb and the appearance of its color."
"Hippocrates does not specify in his book that anything should be placed on the member during its restoration, but rather that a cerote of wax and oil should be made and no other cause recommended; he suggests it should be between thickness and subtlety."
"However, in the case of Galen it is seen that something should be placed on the broken member during its restoration and the causes in which there is heat exhaustion with small heat are such as myrrh, frankincense and similar ones to those we have recommended."
"Method of plaster that relieves injury and pain: take ground mastic, of cypress, of human hairs shaved or feathers of birds, and of althea, and salt, powdered equally; they should be sifted and sieved and are made into a plaster."
"But the quantity according to which the bandage should remain and especially be loosened is that you should consider if there is no pain or pressure nor the bone broken has not moved from its place, then you should not loosen it for many days." "And if there is great pain or pressure or inflammation at that place, then you should cut or loosen it immediately, and remove the plaster from it."
"And take a cloth of linen and a marine sponge dampened, and submerge that in warm water, and with it wash the place within until that pressure and pain are relieved; then let the member rest for an hour; then apply over it clean wool immersed in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and tie it on for one night so that you are sure of a cauterized abscess, and that the inflammation of the member is eased, and the pain from it is removed."
"And then continue that subtle strapping and small plaster until you have healed completely with your first strapping, subtly."
"And if often times the abscess and the heat and the pain and the inflammation and all accidents have been removed from everything, then you should continue that as you did at the beginning, equally."
"But if no cause of those that we have mentioned occurs to the member, then you should not loosen it except after three or four days or five or seven; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days according to what appears to you by the loosening of the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and joins together with the coagulation of the flesh over it."
"And then tighten the strapping more than your first strapping all around, and adjust according to the thickness of the flesh of the sick person, as it has preceded."
"But if you see that the place of the fracture is already dry and exhausted more than it should be, then you know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore, moisten it with warm water every time you loosen it; do this for all three days; and slightly loosen the strapping, and by this operation, bring nourishment to the member, and it will heal easily."
"But what foolish restorers of the fracture of the member do sometimes when it is not restored at the beginning as it should be, and is restored crookedly, is an error according to their operation, great fear, and if it were right, this would have been enlarged by the Ancients in their books and written with that done."
"And I have not seen any of them do that, so it is more reasonable that they should not do it."
Chapter according to: On fracture when it occurs in the head.
"There are many species of fractures, and their figures are diverse, and their causes are distinct. For some fractures happen by percussion of a blow, which crushes all the bone inside the head, leaving the bone bare, just as it happens in wood; this species is called depressed. Or the blow cuts into some part of the bone and cuts the flesh of it but does not pass through to the inner side of it; this species of fracture is called elevating absolute."
"And the plague of these two fractures is great or small, and of fracture that is contusion or abrasion; and the cause of it is percussion with powder or cement on stone, and similar things."
"And this fracture is penetrating, which approaches the pericranium that leaves the bone bare; or it is on the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures is great or small."
"And this fracture is hidden in the bone, in the small subtlety of the hair, and therefore it is called capillary fracture."
"And this fracture happens either by cement or percussion of stone and similar things, which causes some laminas of bone to enter into the inner parts, and it happens in the concavity of the location as it happens with wrenches when they are struck and their entrance enters into the inner parts."
"And this mostly happens in moist bone courses, such as those of infants' heads."
"And so with all these species of fractures, their parts are separated like stars scattered." "And I have brought this in remembrance of all this in its place."
"And you should know all these species of fracture by detection on them and by inquiry about them with tests and odor of rotting flesh that is above them."
"And capillary fracture is recognized by this: it is discovered on the bone and wiped off, and then touched with a finger because the fracture appears black."
"But in the treatment of fracture, consider at the beginning the accidents of the sick person. And then if you see by those accidents that it signifies by manifest sign for fear, such as vomiting from cholera, extension, loss of understanding, syncope, acute fever, and swelling of the eyes with redness of them, similar to them in great dispositions, without doubt it will not be healed."
"And if you see that accidents are not current, then you hope for health. Then begin the treatment of it."
"The reason for this is that if the plague has come to you as it was at first and this happens in the days of winter, then you should study the structure of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition."
"And if it is in the days of summer, then you should remove the bone on the seventh day, having to ensure that what is left of the bone is corrupted by the pericranium and that the accidents that we have mentioned happen."
"And if the fracture has already reached the pericranium covering the brain and is with small parts and abrasion, then you should cut into the comminuted and abraded part, as I will teach you."
"And then shave the head of the sick person according to custom and manner as you can according to the figure of the plague, and let it be light on the sick person."
"And if it happens to you that you must make the detection of the bone, fluxes of blood or hot abscess, then you should pause on it with what is necessary and use cloths infused in wine and rose oil to make the plague rest until the abscess is sedated and you are sure of the flux of blood. And then begin in the circumcision of the bone and in the ablation of it."
"And this should be done according to one of two operative methods."
"And the first method is that you should cut into the bone with an incision of such a shape. And then administer another incision knife a little wider than the first one."
"And then use the incision knife even more wide than the second one."
"You must take various cutting tools with you, among which some are sharper than others. Some are shorter than others and they have sharp points at their ends; they should be made of iron or steel."
"And use them with ease in percussion on the cutting tools, so that you do not cut the head and harm him."
"And if the bone is hard and tough, then you must perforate around it beforehand before you administer the cutting tools with the non-depth saws."
"And these are not called thus but because those do not reach the term of the bone of the testicle near where it is approached by the saw, for at the end of that which is left sharp resembles a small margin or circle, which prevents being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone."
"And it is convenient that you take many of these saws with you, each one suitable for the thickness of each bone. Thus, you will have at hand all kinds of saws for all types of bones in the brain whose ends are sharp and whose length and width vary according to the thickness of this bone."
"And this is the method of three kinds of saws: The quality for piercing around the broken bone is that you place the saw on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the saw to another location, pause where there is between all tracks equal to the thickness of a hair or about that."
"And then incise with the cutting tools between each track of the bone, and make it as subtle as possible at the end of the incision, as we have said before, until you radiate the bone, or with your hand, or with any other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for that purpose, such as tweezers and fine forceps."
"And it is necessary that you guard with all caution that the cutting tools do not touch any part of the brain."
"And then when you have pierced the bone and separated it from the brain if it is connected to it, remove it carefully, scraping and smoothing its roughness with some tool similar to a cutting tool but more blunt and subtle than others."
"And if some small bone fragments remain scattered, then remove them easily with whichever tool seems appropriate to you; and afterwards clean the wound with linens and dressings which we have mentioned."
"And the other method of operation is easier and farther from fear, which Galen describes as worthy of great praise; and this is his saying: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin discovering the broken bone from the place where the fracture is most violent and evident, until when you have discovered that part and paused there, you gently place the end of this soft saw on it whose form is as follows:"
"And the lenticular part is long and does not cut any tissue, and the sharp part is on both sides in length like this: that the lenticular part fits the brain with its face facing the sharp part of the bone."
"And then strike the saw with a small hammer until you have cut all the bone easily as it is turned, and make sure that no panic occurs suddenly in any way, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them, certainly he would be asleep."
"Then if any tissue attached to the bone of the skin remains in some places of the bone, cut it off with the end of that lenticular saw and remove it easily, because it is removed without harm or fear."
"And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the skin, and what is cut off or remains on the bone is its rough surface, then it is necessary to scrape that roughness with fine razors which you have prepared in advance in different quantities so that it will be possible for you to apply them in every place where they are most convenient and most suitable according to how you have proceeded in your operation of the broken bone and its shape."
"And you should administer in your scraping operation at the beginning the broadest of those razors, and then use progressively the more subtle ones, and do not stop doing so until you have administered the most suitable and the most subtle of all."
"But in each of those other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you should use caution according to how you proceed with their correction."
"And this is what has not been written by any of those who have studied that art." "Now we have shown in great fractures what others have written."
"And if the skin is torn when you smooth out the bone, then you should take cloth according to the size of the wound and submerge it in rose oil, and place it on the opening of the wound; and also take another piece of cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil, and place it gently on the first piece without pressing too hard so as not to compress the brain; and then apply a broad bandage and do not wrap it tightly but only according to the size of the wound alone."
"And leave it with this dressing for one or two days until you are sure of cauterization of the wound; and then remove it and apply medications that cause excessive dryness, such as linseed oil and bran flour, powder of frankincense and of aloe, and similar substances."
"And you should make these medications into powders that are sprinkled on the wound while they are dry; and generally speaking, apply to it any medication whose property is to cleanse without biting."
"And make sure that the wound in your treatment is clean and not foul with grease or other dirt, nor do you allow it to become inflamed; because when it settles on the skin of the brain, it corrupts it and poisons it, and causes a serious illness."
"And it happens in the brain of the head when the bone is exposed and even more so when carelessness of it occurs, and blackness appears on its surface; therefore consider now if you have caused any of those accidents that we have mentioned, you would know that they are harmful without doubt."
"And if blackness appears only from the medication that has been applied on top, and it is because of the virtue of that medication that causes that blackness, then you should take a portion of honey and three portions of rose oil, and with them moisten a cloth and place it on the head; and then treat it with whatever remedies are appropriate until it heals."
"Chapter III."
"On the restoration of the nose when it is broken."
"You should know that the nose is not broken except for two parts that are usually elevated together, or one of those sometimes viewed alone, because in it there are two bones; therefore only one of them is cartilaginous and not broken, but you can cause it to become bruised and twisted."
"But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into its socket and use your index finger inside to reduce that fracture, using your thumb outside until the nose returns to its natural shape." "And may you do this with ease, but make sure that you do not cause pain for the patient during your operation."
"If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and it cannot be reached directly, then it should be treated with a probe having a small thickness at its tip."
"And if the fracture is on both sides, you must perform this operation."
"And if it is not possible to restore him on the first day of the fracture, you should wait for 7 or 8 days until the swelling subsides."
"And then insert a strip of cloth into the tip of the nose if the fracture is on one side, or use two gauze pads if it is on both sides, with enough thickness to fill the hollow of the nose."
"And some of the old restorers said that gauze should be inserted folded in its edges and changed every day, but this is not visible to me. However, it is necessary that the strip be inserted into a bandage of wool combed with powdered mallow; and then insert the gauze so that the bone is fixed and the cartilage is strengthened."
"Sometimes, in place of gauze, a piece of linen cloth soaked in warm water is inserted into the nose. This should be done when there is abundant pus and it is wrapped in a linen cloth. It can also be done even if the fracture of the nose is severe, provided that inspiration is not prohibited."
"This procedure may not be necessary for you; if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use gauze instead."
"And if another time a boil appears on the nose, plaster it with cerote or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon."
"But if no boil appears on the outside, then you should plaster it with flour of mustard and powdered fenugreek mixed with albumen of egg; and press it gently with a linen cloth, without touching the nose in any way."
"But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, then you should bind those places and extract them using appropriate instruments; and then stitch up the wound and treat it with something that adheres and consolidates, and use plasters that are appropriate for it."
"And if plague occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with gauze and insert lead cannulae until it is completely healed."
Chapter IV
"On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated."
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture does not heal well, observe whether the fracture is merely displaced and not broken into two pieces, curved inward at the edges. Then its treatment is lighter.
Then, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left jawbone, then insert the said index finger of the right hand and push gently with it inward at the edges of the fracture using the other hand to keep it from slipping outward.
And if the jawbone fracture is already separated into two pieces, then you must apply traction to both parts according to the proper line until it is possible to achieve reduction.
And if there is displacement of teeth due to fracture, then strengthen those that you expect will break with gold, silver or thread wire. Then place a pad over the broken jawbone and another pad over the folded cloth, and place a large steel plate made especially for this purpose, or a wooden wedge along the length of the jawbone. And tie it on top according to how you have prepared the ligature and what is appropriate for the application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to rest quietly, and instruct them not to use suction when eating their food.
And if you estimate that the figure is already altered in some way, then on the third day, remove and untie it. And then correct what has been altered of it, and apply plaster with powder of chalk, albumen of egg, or flour of wheat, having first removed the cerate from it, and cover it with linen cloth.
And then when the plaster is dry and well-adjusted on it, and if the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it in place until the fracture has healed and strengthened. For often this fracture heals in three weeks.
And if another time an abscess occurs, then administer whatever has been said many times in the treatment of it until it is healed and the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is accompanied by inflammation, then consider whether it is due to separation of bone fragments or foreign bodies. If there are foreign bodies, then carefully remove them using whatever is appropriate for removing those instruments.
And if the opening of the inflammation is narrow, then dilate it with the flebotomy probe according to the amount needed.
And then when you have removed those foreign bodies and there remains no trace of them, then close the opening of the inflammation if it is wide. And if not, cover it with one of the plasters that are appropriate for this purpose and bandage it until it has healed.
Chapter Five
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken
Very often the clavicle is fractured at its junction in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways:
Either it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without accident; and it is easy to restore it; or it is such that the accident happened in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is such that the fracture is with plague.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without plague, is to have two ministers present: one should hold the assistant and follow the broken collarbone, while the other extends the neck towards that part.
And then grasp the fracture with your fingers so that it follows the shape of the second one mentioned earlier, and there is neither prominence nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then pause by placing a pillow of wool or cloth under the affected shoulder, its size according to your needs. Then extend and elevate the collarbone and press the pillow with your hand while grasping the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone out to its original position, then have the patient lie on their back and place a folded towel or cloth under their shoulder that is as thick as needed.
And the minister should press on the muscles of the shoulder from outside until the bone of the collarbone is elevated to its original position. Then you can reduce the fracture and grasp it with your fingers as mentioned earlier.
But if you feel sharp pains in the collarbone that are disturbing, then press on it and remove those sharp points with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then make an incision on it with one of the scalpels that you have brought for this purpose. You had placed the scalpel on a table or other suitable surface earlier. The scalpel should be made of wood or iron and its shape is as follows:
And it should resemble a spoon with this concavity. Its width should be according to your needs, depending on the size of the bone or the prominence of it.
But its length should be as short as possible for the operation; it should have two ends like this: one end is wide and the other is narrow.
And if the disruption of the skin that you incised when removing the sharp point from the bone is wide and there is a risk of abscess, then close the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small or you suspect an abscess, then cover the wound with dressings and powders according to the size of the wound.
And if an abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and apply it to the affected area.
And all of this should be done without applying too much pressure on the broken bone. And every day you should examine the patient to see if the bandage has softened or if the board has shifted. If so, then you should correct this and tighten the dressing accordingly.
And also make the patient sleep with their head elevated and place a small cushion on the side of the bed away from them at night. This way, the patient's shoulder will be elevated above the rest of their body and thus the fracture of the collarbone will be lifted by the muscle, or the arm will lie close to the neck.
And do not remove the dressing until around twelve days have passed without any sign of inflammation or abscess at the location.
And also renew the plaster if you see that it is becoming loose, and adjust it according to the tightening and restore it. Leave it in place until it has been restored and the fracture of the collarbone has healed.
And the bone is strengthened and healed around twenty-eight days, although in some cases it can take less time than this, especially in strong men.
Chapter VI
On the restoration of shoulder fractures.
The blade of the scapula is broken at its lateral end and it is not just the blade that is broken but also its margins.
"And when the place is broken - and it is broken in the middle of him - and it is only known by touch, then according to what is the shape of the fracture so should be the preparation of him and reduce that according to its natural figure in every manner that you are able."
"And also pause over the powder millstone with albumen of egg and stop the lion; and also pause with powdered linen doubled; and also pause over him on the left side of the table subtly according to the quantity of the whole spatula or a little more."
"But if the left side is concave in some of the places of the spatula, then you should fill that concavity with egg stopple, and then place the left side according to equality."
"And also stretch above it with a humid cloth with proper constriction and firmness of the left side within that it may not be removed from its place."
"And consider the ligature every day; and always whenever it is softened, then on another day stretch it and make the left side equal if it has been removed from its place."
"And dress the wound upon its own side."
"And the bandage is renewed every 20 days or at most 25."
"And then when this number is completed, then you should dissolve the ligature, and be sure that that is of the bones whose union was not impeded nor their dissolution."
"And if you proceed from the place of the weight and leave the artery there, then cut upon it, and stay it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the clavicle concerning the drainage of an abscess if it happens here."
"Chapter VII"
"On the restoration of a broken foot."
"The foot sometimes breaks in the middle of it and this is quite serious, but its ends usually remain intact."
"And when the middle of the foot breaks, it happens that it sinks into its parts, causing great pain and difficulty in walking, coughing, and sometimes vomiting blood. And this is evident inside the broken bone and cannot be hidden from sensation."
"And the restoration of it is that the patient should lie on their back and place between the palms of their hands a cushion; they should press lightly on the muscles of the foot, align its bones with those of two other toes, perform the operation carefully and correctly according to every possible manner and what is appropriate for this, until the bone returns to its normal shape."
"And then apply a plaster and lint and then place it on a thin board of linen or calico, which you should wrap in cloth. And carefully wrap that around the broken bone so that it is not moved; and sometimes bind it to the back with bandages according to its shape, and tighten it with proper strapping. And check the binding every hour, tightening it when it becomes loose."
"And if it becomes necessary to remove that, you must do so with care or pressure, pain, or abscess occurring in the place. Then you should cut and untie that, remove the plaster, and correct whatever happened there according to its manner of cure; and then reapply the plaster, sometimes changing its face; and adjust the strapping until it is healed."
Capítol viii
"Chapter VIII: Restoration of the spine when broken."
You know that fracture of the ribs occurs only in large spaces that are located on the back, and at the extremities of their ends near the point where it happens it does not cause such injury except by that which is cartilage-like. And its discovery is not hidden from feeling through its examination with these [tools].
And the restoration should be done in such a way that it aligns the fracture using these tools according to the most possible method, ensuring that it is well aligned where necessary, and then apply plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel if needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is leaning towards its interior ends, then intense pain and piercing occur to the sick person, similar to the piercing that occurs to one who has coughed, and because the bone pierces the pleura; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting a lot of blood; and this is difficult to treat.
And already the Ancients knew about that with many tools.
"And they say this: whenever pauses are made in the feeding of the sick that causes inflation and windiness, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed outwards."
"May we avoid this, so that it does not lightly cause an abscess to form, if it has not already happened; but if it has already happened, then it will help with that."
"And some of them say that the wind should be paused and pressed down so that it comes out strongly; but this is similar to reasoning, yet it is feared that the wind will drive the superfluities to the place due to weakness."
"And some of them say: the place should be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be paused between the ribs until it is filled up so that the bandage fits well when wrapped around it according to its roundness; and the illness should be treated with food and medicinal pleurisy cure."
"And if the illness causes violent constriction which cannot be endured, and the bone pierces the membrane with a piercing blow, then they fear for the illness. Therefore, it is necessary to open up over the place, uncover the broken rib, and immediately place upon that instrument which preserves the hip joint, the remembrance of which has been preceded, and insert the bone with ease, and remove it."
"And also, if the wound is large, we should bring its two lips together with stitches, and treat it with poultices until it is healed."
"And if an abscess forms, then quickly take fine powders and mix them with rose oil, place them on the spot, and treat the illness with what is appropriate for the internal abscess."
"And let him be propped up on pillows while he sleeps, until he is healed."
"Chapter ix."
"On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck."
"Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and it is difficult to reduce them; similarly with the vertebrae of the spine." "And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: for if the sinews are already softened, stunned, and deadened, and he cannot move those limbs nor extend nor stretch them nor contract them at all; and when you compress those parts with your fingers or prick them with a needle and there is no pain felt in those parts, then you will know that it will not be healed, and this condition is mortal."
"And if those parts are moved and there is felt in them compression with the fingers or pricking with a needle, then you will know that the nape of the bone is saved, and that the illness will recover with treatment."
"And if you see a similar condition in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider his pulse."
"Then if you find that the sinews are already softened, it is as was said concerning the limbs; and also when he lies on his back, there is shortness of breath and involuntary evacuation of excrement, and sometimes he becomes prostrate on his back and wants to urinate but cannot. Then you will know that it is mortal, and do not bother with treating him."
"But if none of these causes occur, then the case is better and easier to treat."
"And the treatment for this is to make an incision over the hard abscess which has formed on the displaced vertebra, using rose oil alone or with honey; place some of this mixture upon it several times a day until the hard abscess is softened."
"And then apply one of the soothing and stimulating poultices, and compress it with a bandage. And order the patient to rest quietly and not sleep on the painful part until he is healed."
"And if a fracture of the bone is suspected or there is some separation from it, then it must be exposed at the neck and that bone reset. And also approximate the two lips of the wound with sutures if it is large; and treat it with some ointment of honey until it is healed."
"But if the end of the bone is broken off, and this is the end of the coccyx, then you must insert the little finger of your left hand into the anus, and grasp the broken bone with your other hand according to what is possible by means of traction. And then apply a poultice and bandage, if necessary, and compress it."
"And if you suspect broken sinews within it, then open that part and extract those bones, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned until it is healed."
"Chapter 10"
"On the Reduction of Dislocations"
The bones of the broad hips are broken and if they break then their fracture is nothing but a communion in the extremities of those which are inserted into length and decline towards the inside parts; and it happens to the patient pain in that place and punctuation, and the thigh becomes stunned when it breaks.
And its restoration is that you stretch your hand over that entrance which is above the fracture which is its shape.
And if the fracture is only at the end of it then you will shape that according to what appears equal in shape by shaping an entrance that is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is long or already declines towards the left parts then you will make the patient lie on their stomach entrance that has been prepared for you to restore that fracture. And when you have shaped that then pause on the plaster and also pause on that stick of wood or bone, and stretch by tightening over that which you should not fear punctuation of fracture nor movement of the splint.
And shape the concavity with that which fills that entrance where that took the tightening according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their side.
But if an abscess occurs then you will cease the extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is sedated, and pause on that which subdues it according to what has been said above; and also return to the restoration of it, and stretch it thus as is necessary.
And if this happens in the bone that is broken or crushed then you will leave them alone.
Chapter xi
Of the restoration of the fracture of the jawbone.
The jawbone is that which is between the cheek and to the head of the shoulder.
"If it is broken, then its restoration is by one of two methods: the first being that you take a piece of flat board with moderate thickness in this shape:"
"And there are two pieces at both ends, and it is suspended from an elevated place."
"And the patient should sit on a chair, and then place their broken arm on the board so that it reaches the middle of its curvature."
"And then suspend some weight on top of that, or the assistant stretches it towards its parts, and the doctor sets the fracture with his hands together, so that the fracture returns to its proper position."
"And the other method is that the patient lies on their back and suspends their broken arm with a sling, then commands two assistants to each hold one end of the fracture with their hands, and each stretches towards their part."
"And if you want to stretch it further, then place another rope over the fracture and another one on top of that, and each assistant stretches towards their part."
"And if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then place half of the rope on the curvature and the other half below the fracture towards the elbow."
"And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then place the rope on that location and over the elbow."
"And then apply it gently and without difficulty, and as soon as it is applied properly according to what is needed and with good application, then bind it tightly so that it does not happen to have an abscess."
"But if an abscess forms afterwards, then leave the bandage loose for about seven days, and place a compress of clean lint moistened with hot water and rose oil on it until the abscess subsides, and then bind it tightly."
"And the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap it with a layer of clean cloth over that; and then adjust the arm in that position, and place his hand covered over the affected muscle."
"And then wrap it with cloth and bind it tightly around that support for the arm so that the arm remains in place until some cause prevents the bone from breaking."
"And then use splints."
"And then place over the fracture that splint which is broader and stronger than the others, and place it between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on each side."
"And then bind it tightly over the splints with the bandage which was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter."
"And ensure that your bandage is as tight as possible around the location of the fracture." "And when the fracture has healed, then make the bandage looser."
"And therefore if you change the situation of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its normal position, then do so."
"And if you fear an abscess forming, then leave the bandage and splints loose for seven days as we have said."
"And also consider the bandage in all cases for three days to ensure that it does not press too hard or cause inflammation, and therefore adjust the bandage accordingly so that food does not reach the limb due to an excess of tightness, and therefore correct everything as we have said."
"But if you are in doubt about this, then do not untie the bandage for three days or more."
"And let his sleep be on his back and let his hands be on his stomach."
"And place a bolster full of wool under the elbow and ensure at all times that neither day nor night the figure of the broken limb is dislocated or the bandage becomes loose or moistened, and therefore adjust everything with your own skill."
"And provide the patient's food according to the order which we have mentioned earlier, ensuring that the food is softened at first until he begins to recover his strength, and then make it thicker for him."
"As to the custom of the splints and the bedding, they should be tightly bound for forty days, and then they should be untied and bathing should be administered and they should be treated with ointments which will correct them."
"And if the fracture is completely healed, then do not untie the bandage and splints for seven days or until two months have passed."
Chapter XII
On the restoration of fractures of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the two "focils"; one of which is small and it lies on the side of the thumb, and the other is large and lies next to the little finger on its outer side.
And sometimes only the larger "focil" is broken, or sometimes only the smaller one, but most often they are both broken at the same time.
And when the small "focil" is broken above, then its restoration will be easier and its healing will also be quicker.
And when it is broken below, the fracture is bad and difficult to heal.
And worse still is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the small "focil" above, then it is advisable for the physician to perform a simple extension with ease until that part becomes swollen.
But if the large "focil" is broken, then strong extension should be used.
And if both "focils" are broken at the same time, then you must use strong traction with care.
And it is necessary that you place the figure of the hand in its proper position during restoration and extension, with the wrist stretched out on a bolster, and let the thumb be on top of all the other fingers, and let the little finger be lower than the others, and let the patient sit on a chair covered with a blanket.
And place a bolster before him at his height or towards his right side so that the patient does not stretch out too much.
And moreover let one assistant extend the arm from below with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above in the same way.
And then let the physician swell the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if during the fracture reduction you are unsuccessful, then use all your skill to reduce everything back into its proper place.
And if during the reduction you find that the elbow is separated and not reduced properly, then use force on it and reset it according to the method which we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is with dislocation, I have already dealt with this in another chapter, and therefore take care of it according to that method.
And if an abscess forms at the beginning of your restoration, then take a cloth smeared with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, which is between specificity and subtlety, and place it on the area with slight pressure until the abscess bursts. Then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from powdered mallow with clear honey, and also apply splints; and let the splint which is placed over that fracture be wide and strong.
And know that in the case of fractures of the arm there are usually six splints used, or seven if the fracture is in both bones at once.
And then apply your traction with force and vehemence over the area of the fracture, and especially if there is strong tension above or below the fracture site, apply gentle traction beforehand according to what was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
"Let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft moistened ones, neither hard nor rough, and let the oil with which it is strained be purely middle-ground between fine and coarse, as we have advised."
"And consider the limb and the wound after a few days." "And if any cause arises that necessitates correction, such as swelling in the limb, then you should immerse the limb in cold water until the swelling ceases."
"Leave the limb to rest without constraint, and then gradually increase the strapping."
"And if the strapping has been softened and the bone has set well enough, then correct everything with your own skill."
"Also consider whether nourishment is being hindered from reaching the limb due to excess strapping. If so, you should loosen it slightly and leave it for a few days until nourishment can reach it again, then tighten it."
"But if the illness is not affected by any of the causes mentioned above, then it should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts."
"And then have the patient hang their arm by its own strength of movement and make them sleep on their back."
"Know that this fracture is typically restored in thirty or thirty-two days, but it may also be restored in twenty-eight days. This depends on the disposition of complexions and the dispositions of strength."
Chapter XIII
"On the restoration of fractures of the thumb and fingers."
"Know that in the palm of the hand and toes of these people there is often fracture, not just bruising many times seen."
"And when the hand has fracture or bruising, it is necessary that the patient lie on their back and have their head hang down level with their chest, and near pause the hand over that extended one."
"And then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician aligns them together in good alignment."
"And then it is necessary to apply plaster and gauze, if there is no cauterization; and then place a splint on top according to the quantity of the location, and you have already wrapped that in linen cloth."
"But if the fracture is in the palms of the hands inside the belly of the palm, then make a similar splint of cloth, and command the patient to hold that with their hand clenched; then it should be wrapped with long cloth."
"And let the splint be of leather, with cloth laced onto it, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and hold it according to what is necessary."
"But if the fracture is on the outside of the hand, then place splints above and below the affected part of the palm, so that the hand is straight and upright; and then apply the dressing, just as it would be wrapped around the wrist between those parts."
"And if there is a fracture of any of the joints of those fingers, then if it is the thumb, align it as necessary and then hold it with the palm; and if you wish, place a small splint on top to elevate the fracture and prevent it from moving."
"But if the fracture is in other fingers, such as that of the middle joint, or the index finger, or the ring finger, then align and bind them with the finger that is adjacent to it; or let them all be bound continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on top, just as we said for the thumb."
"And consider, in your setting and application of the dressing, the position of the abscess, cauterization and counterpressure - according to what is necessary when such a cause occurs, following the narrative described above."
"Chapter XIV."
"On the reduction of fractures of the bone of the leg."
"The bone of the leg is often fractured, and it becomes evident to the touch as it turns towards the front of the joint and the pus forms above it."
"And its reduction is done as follows: a bandage is applied above the fracture and another below it, and the patient lies on their stomach and face."
"And then the practitioner extends all the ligaments to their proper place when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone."
"But if it is displaced towards the knee joint, then pull the ligament attached to the tendon of the calf muscle upwards, so that its extension goes upwards and the other bandage below the fracture."
"And similarly if the fracture is displaced towards the ankle joint, then pull the ligament attached to the tendo
"And then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it reduces according to its natural shape and the bone is joined by good union. Then it is necessary to apply a plaster cast and splint if there is no abscess in the limb." "But if there is an abscess present, then wait for a few days until the abscess is resolved by cauterization, and then return to your treatment."
"The splinting of it is done as follows: wrap a hard bandage around the fracture and fold it two or three times, removing any excess from it. Then wrap the leg so that it reaches the ankle and knee joint."
"And insert a long pin between the leg and the bandage near the ankle joint on both sides, and bring the ends of the pin from above the two parts. Then wrap the excess of the bandage around the leg and foot."
"And then place a splint on the leg at the location of that fracture, and also place one on the bone of the shin."
"And then fill any spaces between the leg and bandage with soft cloths, so that the structure is stabilized."
"And then begin with strong binding in the middle above the location of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and the more you stretch the bandage at the location of the fracture, the less tight your binding will be and the lighter and softer it will become."
"And then extend both ends of the pin outwards on both sides until it reaches the heel of the foot. Then tie both ends of the splints tightly on the other side to ensure that the bandage does not shift from its position."
"And then leave the splint on for about three weeks, provided that there is no gangrene, cauterization of the abscess, inflammation or anything similar. But if any such cause occurs, then be careful and remove it immediately, and correct whatever happens right away, as we have said many times."
"But if there is a bone spur that presses on the bone and causes pain, then align it as much as possible. And if you cannot do this, then file down the spur and remove it, and treat it with whatever preceded the removal until it heals."
"And sometimes this fracture of the leg is strengthened with splints other than those mentioned earlier, as we have described in the case of the arm."
"But nevertheless, in the restoration of it in this way, it does not happen that the patient limps. And if it is restored alone, without applying the other leg, then it is necessary for the patient to limp all the time."
"And know that the leg is strengthened in about forty days or a little more or less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions."
Capítulo XV.
About the restoration of fractures of the ankle joint.
"Know that fractures of the ankle joint are rare, but they happen frequently with twisting."
"But if it happens that there is a fracture of the ankle joint, then it is only a crack or tear in its parts, and this is with much or little pain, and everything is judged by sensation."
"And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts should be aligned with these so that it is joined and united as much as possible by means of traction, ease and manufacture."
"And then apply a plaster cast and apply a round splint if necessary, and strengthen it above with bandages as needed."
"And also consider all the dispositions that we have recommended for other fractures, such as abscesses and similar things so that it is prevented from any accident with whatever is appropriate until it heals."
Capítulo XVI.
About the restoration of fractures of the leg bone
"Know that in the leg there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called by name the shinbone, and the other is thin and is called the fibula."
"And it happens to each of the species of fractures what happens to the two bones of the arm, and for that reason the restoration of it is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm by alignment, and the operation of it is one."
"And if both bones are broken at the same time, then the shinbone is crushed at all its parts."
"And if only the thinner bone is broken, then the leg is affected anteriorly in its parts."
"If the bone is too thick, bypass it and search for other causes that are not immediately apparent."
"And apply traction, extension, and ligation of the bones according to that arrangement."
"However, cover it if the fracture of the leg has many settings; then traction should be milder and lighter, and you should proceed with caution."
"And if there is any cause for operation on the arm, and this happens when you are arranging the bones and completing your entire operation, then you will take pieces of wood from a pile that has been placed nearby, made from palm branches; choose those that are not too thick nor too thin, and their length should be equal to the length of the leg, with one end entering inside the other."
"And then wrap it around each bone with two wrappings according to its length, and place one on the lower end of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, so that they are inside the bones."
"And then tie the two bones together in three places on both sides and in the middle, because with this structure, the leg is prevented from bending to the left or right side, and thus it remains in good position."
"And then place a splint made of wood according to the length of the leg, and place it there so that the bone may preserve its mobility."
"This is especially necessary when dealing with fractures, particularly those that are displaced."
"And then examine the leg every day and study it carefully to ensure that there is no inflammation or swelling or other similar causes; if any such cause occurs, then you should apply whatever treatment is necessary for its healing."
"Chapter xvii."
"On fractures of the heel bone and the bones of the foot."
"A fracture of the heel bone does not occur frequently; however, fractures of the bones of the foot do occur sometimes; and those of the toes rarely happen, except for dislocations in their joints."
"If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones become elevated above each other, then the person will limp with their heel on the ground prepared in this way."
"And then place one foot on what is elevated among those bones, press down on them, compress and dislocate them until they return to their proper places."
"And then apply an emplaster and bandage it, and place under the belly of the foot a small table with two flat heads on which to rest."
"And then stretch that [the leg] downwards with the foot, because you have wrapped it with cloth and other means which are necessary."
"And when three or four days have passed, then untie the bandages, because you will find the bones set, either joined together, compressed or dislocated, and the operation on them is as follows."
"But if some of those bones are compressed, then restore them and dislocate them as I have taught you in the restoration of the fingers." "And then place the compressed bone on a splint according to the length of the bone, and leave it there for a few days; and then place that foot on this table which I have taught you about, and stretch it downwards."
"But if what is compressed among the bones are two or three or more, then place on each finger a splint according to its quantity wrapped in linen cloth, and stretch the table towards the lower part of the foot, with one of the flat heads of the table being under the belly of the foot so that it has good retention."
"And you should remember not to neglect considering all the causes which I have mentioned to you regarding the other fractures of accidents that we have discussed; and it is necessary for you to attend to each accident according to what is appropriate."
"Chapter xviii."
"On fractures of the pubic bone, penis bone and clitoris of women and the penile bone of men."
"When the pubic bone of a woman is compressed, then perform a cesarean section on her, incline her slightly backwards at the hips, and hold her there."
"And then the midwife should push the pubic bone with her hand, and it should be done on the bone as if she were feeling for something; and then the woman should be shaken, and slowly elevate her hips upwards, because all that is forgotten until the front of the pubic bone is before the opening and the compressed bone returns."
"And then apply dust on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cloth gently and let her urinate, reduce and return that [the bone] according to the manner in which it was placed at the beginning, and then return her to her first position."
"And do this for seven or eight days until it is restored."
"And if you wish, take a bladder of sheep and stretch it over the wound with a reed cannula; insert all of the bladder into the pubic bone and then insufflate through the cannula with strength until the bladder is inflated inside the pubic bone, because the fracture will return."
"And then fill it with cotton and let it rest for some days as we have recommended until it is healed."
"And when the penile bone of a man or woman is compressed, then administer in its restoration and stitching that which we have recommended for the hipbone."
"And that which is right is not concealed in these fractures nor is it difficult to find, especially for those who have some study and use this my book; because that fracture means to you the greatest arrangement of a straight line in the restoration and stretching of it."
"But in the clitoris of a man when it is compressed, take a gourd of squash and place the clitoris inside; then apply over it wrapping with cloth and wrap it up, and let it rest for three or four days until it is healed."
"Chapter xix."
"On restoration of fractures of the bones when they are with swelling."
"It is convenient for those who suffer fractures with swelling, especially if the bone is large such as the thighbone or shinbone, or similar to that one which you should lance and perform phlebotomy on immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy are present according to what we have said above."
"And if the swelling flows with blood, then it is necessary for you to cauterize its retention in that way that you powder over it with hot wax if there is no other cause present."
"And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay except in case of an aposteme (abscess)."
"But if an abscess forms, then you should delay the restoration of it until the ninth day until the abscess is softened, and do not approach it in the third or fourth day of all because you will cause bad accidents."
"But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin openly, then it is necessary for you to reduce that and align it with your hands gently and with little extension."
"But if extension is not prepared for you and the reduction of it with your hands and cloths, then reduce it with this instrument; and it is an instrument made of iron, seven or eight digits long and its width according to the quantity of swelling; and for that reason it is necessary for the physician to take three or four according to the quantity that is needed for that in the treatment of all kinds of fractures."
"Let him be returned, and let there be little force used lest he be doubled under the pressure at that time of operation."
"The tip should be sharp and well-shaped; it should rise above its base with the greatest thickness and strength entering into the divisions."
"This is how it looks:"
"It is called 'labre volentz atelarem' in Greek, meaning a small plate."
"Why should the extremity of the hand be elevated above the prominence of the bone?"
"And press it back into place with some force."
"And then force the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end is thin and does not fit well with the instrument, insert the tip of that bone into the instrument so it has better grip."
"But if you cannot reduce the bone with what has been recommended, then cut it using the appropriate scissors which we have mentioned earlier; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever manner is possible for you."
"And finally, rasp away any rough edges and small splinters from the bone that remain."
"And when the bone is reduced and the fracture has been properly aligned with minimal pain to the patient, you will know that the bone has returned to its natural position. And then proceed to what you would do for the great benefit of the patient."
"When your reduction of the bone is complete, and then immerse a bandage in black stiptic wine, and especially if it is summer time, do not allow any grease or other substance containing oil to remain on the skin, so that no infection may occur afterward."
"And also apply bandages at the time of completion of the bone setting, and leave the skin uncovered long enough to insert splints with forceps and use wrappings according to the amount of the dressing."
"And be careful that you do not stretch the skin over the fracture, for many times this has been done by foolish physicians, and it has caused great harm to their patients, even death or gangrene or other serious diseases. Therefore, beware of this." "And if your bandage is light and soft, different from the usual bandages used for fractures."
"If the dressing is large or bad, and you fear that one of those unfortunate accidents might occur which we have mentioned earlier, and if there is pain in the area, then it is not necessary to use splints. Instead, apply a wrapping of strong cloths in place of bandages and stretch it tightly with them."
"And when after one or two days the dressing begins to get wet, and there is some discharge from it, then remove the bandage that you had placed on it with wine, and apply compresses as usual for dressings, such as tetrafarmacon or similar ones."
"And it is advisable for you to remove the splints and examine the dressing every morning and evening until the bone has consolidated and healed properly. And also prepare the limb in such a way that the gangrene may flow from it towards its lower parts easily."
"If many days have passed and the dressing is not consolidated nor separated from the bone, then you should know that there are some small arteries at work here, for which you must examine the dressing with a probe." "And of those small arteries which are separated and removed, those which cannot be separated nor cause pain or swelling in the limb, pierce them with a needle according to what is possible for you."
"And if you find something hot or cold in the dressing, such as gangrene or other kinds of corruption and putrefaction, then you should pause for one day with that which is described in its chapter concerning the cure of this matter."
"And in this matter, my speech must be brief and you must perform your task: when the bone is crushed, do not elevate it above the limb like the bones of the foot and hand or those of large limbs. Do not attempt to reduce it nor extract it because many times death has occurred due to this. Leave that alone until it decays naturally, as it will spontaneously in about twenty or thirty days. Then treat the dressing if you can see it, otherwise leave it alone."
"Chapter 20."
"Of the treatment of inflammation that occurs in the vicinity of some fracture."
Broken bones often heal poorly with scarring, especially near joints, affecting the limb's function.
If the fracture is fresh, apply medicines that have astringent properties like aloe and senna. Then examine it, seek its cause, observe its appearance, and apply some or all of these remedies with wine as an adhesive, albumen of egg whites, or vinegar. Apply them on linen over the fracture and bind them tightly.
"Leave the bandage untouched for several days; then remove it and replace with another until swelling is gone; or apply a lead sheet descendant on it, as lead has property to reduce all that is inflamed in the limbs."
"If the swelling has already turned into hard tissue and it is necessary to remove it carefully, then cut through it along the upper part of it and incise the excess and prominence. Then dress it with some compresses until healed."
