Capítulo XXII
"Regarding the treatment of bones that have healed crookedly and prohibit their correction."
"When an injury occurs to a previously restored member during its healing process due to crookedness and prominence of the bone broken and knotted, and it becomes inflamed in that form, but still retains its natural operation, you should not accept the claim that the member has been recently re-broken."
"And yet there are many foolish physicians and healers who have done this in our land, and this operation is greatly to be condemned, leading to great fear of the signal which proves fatal."
"But if limpness and knotting are recent, then they should be fomented with hot water containing herbs which have a softening effect, such as mallows, rose hips, marshmallow root, and similar ones; and poultices should be applied using mollifying plasters made in this way or using mucilage of mallow root mixed with grease of goose or oil of camomile, and plastered on; or else figs fried in fat should be trussed with feathers of pigeons, and such like medications which are named 'softeners'."
"And sometimes they are dissolved with continuous gentle friction using the most humid ones, and motion of the member should be administered in all parts at all hours."
"But if the tortuosity has already appeared and is strong and hardened into bone, and there is a need for cure of it with iron, then it is necessary to cut as much of the excess as possible and dissolve the continuity of the bone, and what is superfluous in the nodosity of that member should be excised with fine scissors, and ease should be given to that with solicitude and care."
Chapter XXIII
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some of the joints of the limb, causing prohibition of movement and distortion of the figure of the member, and bringing about great pain and injuries.
Therefore when dislocation occurs, it is necessary to promptly endeavor that it be reduced, and not delay its reduction, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hardened, making the reduction of dislocation difficult.
And for this reason it is not necessary to move or stretch the joint at the time of inflammation, for often this brings about spasm and severe pains.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you apply the phlebotomy of the disease, and immediately let the inflammation subside; and then the member should be embrocated with hot water and oil, and then reduced gently.
And every limb should be treated with that which brought about its dislocation in the first place.
And I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what was said earlier about fracture, from the most superior part of the body down to the most inferior.
Chapter XXIV.
"Regarding the treatment of dislocation of the jaw."
"If both jaws are dislocated and not aligned; the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their positions due to minor movement and become softened somewhat; or they are completely dislocated, with the joint being relaxed at the point of separation such that saliva flows from the patient's mouth and they cannot close it or speak, with their tongue wrapped inside their cheek."
"However, when the dislocation is slight, he returns to his usual position on his own without any cause."
"But if the dislocation is complete and full, it must be gently reduced immediately, and not delayed at all."
"And the physician should hold the patient's head, and insert his index finger into the depression of the dislocation on one side inside the mouth if it is only on that side; or he should insert both his thumbs together if it is on both sides, with the other fingers placed outside to steady him; and he commands the patient to close their jaws and keep them still; and the physician gently replaces the jaw and presses it until it returns to its proper position."
"And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then evaporation with hot water and oil should be administered until it becomes easy to reduce them both without delay."
"And once they have returned and are engaged, if the patient's mouth remains closed and not softened, then apply a plaster with powdered lint mixed with wax and rose oil; let it be made of wax and beaten egg white; and apply it with ease using linen cloths."
"And let the patient sleep on their back with their head supported between two pillows so that they do not move their head to the right or left; and do not force them to eat anything until after the pain has subsided and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want."
"And this should be administered with ease, and not force the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking until the jaw is healed."
"And if the reduction of two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated and not returned to their proper places at any time, then often bloodletting and frequent sudorifics are administered, and sometimes vomiting is induced by drinking a purgative."
"And when you do this, know that it is harmful; and often the person to whom this happens dies within ten days."
"Chapter .25"
Of the reduction of dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
"The clavicle is not dislocated inward because it is connected to the chest bone; however, sometimes it can be dislocated outward, which can be felt."
"And the reduction of this is that the patient should lie on their back and extend their arms." Then compress the area with your palm using strong pressure, for it will return.
Then pause on that plaster and powders and strengthen it.
If the end of that which seizes the cord continues with it without being dislocated on any day, then it is necessary to reduce and wrap it according to what we have said and according to what appears to you.
Then pause on that plaster, powders, and bandage, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until they are healed.
And with this same treatment, the end of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI
Of the reduction of dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle can be dislocated in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated at the parting of the chest and the parting of the joints.
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and that is rarely done; and it is not dislocated at the parts behind by the shoulder blade; and it is not dislocated at the parts in front by the nerves.
And more often it is dislocated and exits outwards at the parts of the joints near the chest, especially in those whose flesh is scarce, it is easily dislocated; but in those with much flesh, it exits outwards with difficulty.
And sometimes a severe blow or fall happens to some men, and the muscle becomes inflamed with an incurable inflammation, and it is injured in such a way that it has already become dislocated.
Therefore you should first examine him thoroughly about it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is at the parts near the chest. Therefore you should distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the intact one, because you will find a clear difference between them. And you will find concavity in the head of the muscle towards the chest, like a hollow, and in the sick person, they cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor can they move it with any movement.
If it is dislocated towards the foot or upwards, you should then find a clear touch and not hidden.
When this dislocation occurs, it can be easily reduced if it's fresh and the patient is a child.
To reduce it, the minister raises the patient's hand above their head; then quickly pushes down on their shoulders to bring them back into place, using force if necessary. The minister then raises their own hand and extends it over theirs as they return gently to their original position.
But if it does not return with this method, and several days have passed since the dislocation occurred, then the patient should be bathed in hot water and given a plaster to soften and smooth the area, made from ingredients such as boiled comfrey leaves, flaxseed, and milk of almonds.
Then the patient should be propped up on their back and held firmly in place with their arms elevated slightly higher than their head. The doctor places their foot on the patient's sternum, lifts the detached muscle head with force, and pulls the patient's arm towards them to prevent it from being pulled out of place again. Another assistant should hold the patient's head to keep it steady while this is done.
Alternatively, you can reduce the dislocation using this method: Have someone present who is taller than the patient and stand on the same side as the dislocated shoulder. Insert their muscle behind the patient's clavicle and lift it up to its proper position while the patient is suspended in mid-air. Another assistant should pull the patient's arm away from their stomach to prevent it from moving out of place again. But if the patient is lighter, then another cause should be suspended alongside them, so that they may be weighted down, as this helps to prevent the dislocation from returning.
And it can also be reduced in this way: Have a long pole with a round, mortar-like head placed on the ground. Have the patient sit on top of it with their back to the pole, then have them lean forward and extend their arms out to the side, using their healthy arm for support. Then, with force, they should straighten up and extend their torso towards the other side. The joint will quickly return to its proper place.
And if reduction of the dislocation proves difficult despite all the methods mentioned, then use this treatment: Take a pole that is two spans long and four fingers wide. Hollow out its thickness according to this pattern:
"Then also place lion's head cloths on top of the patient to prevent them from moving."
"Then place your hand on the patient's chest, extend it over their body towards their sides, and lie the body down on the helper and your arm and hand."
"Then place the arm on a step stool according to its width, extend your hand towards the patient's sides, and hang the other side from the other side because the joint is close."
"And when the patient's treatment is complete according to your best ability, then place on their chest a lanolin compress of equal quantity; also apply a plaster prepared from ground millet and egg whites over all the muscles, such that it covers them completely; and then bind the compress in place with a suitable bandage. The limb should be wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's hand should be attached to their neck, and left in this position for nine days without moving."
"And it is necessary that the patient's diet be restricted while the limb is being cared for, because it is easier for healing to occur when the member is lighter."
"And then increase the patient's diet after seven or eight days and encourage movement; but if it is still not firm and flexible, then it has not yet healed."

And know that the joint will never be properly fixed in place."

"If the condition worsens and there is delay in limb movement, then the patient should use many warm baths until that heaviness is relieved and returns to its initial state."
Here's the translation of Chapter XXVII from "The Decameron" by Giovanni Boccaccio:
"On the treatment of dislocation of the hip."
"You should know that the joint of the hip is dislocated with difficulty and returns to its place with difficulty; and that one which has been dislocated in all directions is more difficultly reduced than one which has only been dislocated forwards or backwards, and the dislocation is not hidden from your view when it is either prominent or depressed according to whatever figure it may be dislocated in, but when you have replaced the dislocated hip joint into its place, this is clearly indicated to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the thigh nor touch with that muscle."
"And if you do not reduce it and the dislocation comes to be followed by suppuration, then reduction becomes difficult and cure is altogether uncertain; and especially when it has been displaced backwards, for this is the worst of all species of dislocation and causes the greatest pain, and many times death ensues from it."
"And the method of reducing it when possible is to extend the limb with two persons and expand the arm, place one's hands on the joint and push it back into its place with the palms of both hands together or with the edge of the palm turned inwards."
"But if it is dislocated forwards, then the limb should be brought back in this way: bending the arm at the elbow with the hand on the chest, strike the joint with the edge of the palm."
"And if reduction is not accomplished by gentle pressure, then apply violent traction and much force."
"And two persons should extend the arm, and hold it firmly in that position with both hands so that it is not removed during traction."
"And then rotate the arm outwards entirely, for a length of folded bandage or linen is wrapped about its hand."
"And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil that may facilitate its lubrication and aid in reduction; and then press the joint violently with both hands until it returns."
"And after the reduction has been made, it should be kept in place by strapping it up with adhesive plaster and bandages, with a suspension sling made of soft cloth attached to the neck, and it should be allowed to rest for several days."
"And then if the joint is formed in its proper place, untie the bandages from it and let it rest."
"But if the joint is not strong enough, renew the adhesive plaster and bandages, and let them remain for a few days until strength returns; then remove them."
"But if severe pain occurs during the reduction of the joint and the limb is in motion, then apply a warm bath and gentle friction and compression until relieved. Or place over the joint an alleviating poultice, especially one composed of linseed oil; and then bind it up gently and leave it on overnight and during the day." "And then remove the poultice and put the limb in a bath; and when it is warm, gently rub the joint with friction and apply the alleviating poultice again, which is a linseed oil plaster, once on the first day, twice on the second day, and three times on the third day." "and repeat this process while bathing until it is healed."
"And if desired, apply to the joint a wet poultice made from crushed cabbage leaves bordered with soft cloth and compress it gently. Do this several times until the poultice is dry and the inflammation has subsided."
"Chapter XXVIII."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the malleolus, that is of the joint between the wrist and the hand."
"Dislocation of the malleolus occurs sometimes when the hand is injured; and its reduction is easy, unlike that of other joints. But care should be taken to reduce it promptly before inflammation develops or an abscess forms."
"And the reduction of this dislocation is accomplished by placing the patient's hand on a table with the palm facing down, and then the assistant extends the fingers while the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint and presses it until it returns to its proper position."
"But it is important to note that if the palm of the hand is already on the stomach side of the hand, then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is on the ulnar side, then the position of the hand should be with the palm facing downwards on the table, so that the physician's hand can cover the prominence of the joint."
"And then if it returns quickly, well and good. But if not, compress the joint with adhesive plaster to prevent inflammation and let it rest. And do not repeat this procedure more than necessary, because if it is not reduced promptly within several days, it will become difficult to treat."
"But the joint may remain stiff due to its tortuosity; and do not inform the patient of this, but tell him that his hand is paralyzed and he cannot move it for some reason. Then you should know that the nerves are compressed or cut, and then you should treat it by cauterizing around the opening of the joint and applying heat afterwards."
"So when dislocation has been reduced, then apply the plaster which we have recommended, and let it remain for five days. Then remove it and let the hand rest for several more days before beginning to exercise it."
"But if there is still some restriction of motion and pain occurs in the joint, then apply a cold compress and gentle friction several times until it is relieved."
"Chapter XXIX."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the fingers."
"Sometimes the fingers are completely dislocated at their bases."
"And when one of the fingers is dislocated on its dorsal or palmar side, then extend the finger and press the dislocation back into place with your thumbs until it returns."
"And then support the head of the finger, suspend that finger downwards at the side where it is dislocated, and let it remain for two days."
"And then suspend it and extend it until the finger is straight on its own accord within that day."
"And when night comes, bind the finger according to this description and do not cease stretching it during the day and exercising with motion. And bind it at night for several days until it is straight."
"And do the same if a finger is dislocated on its palmar side. Bind it to that position and perform your first operation until it is healed."
"And do likewise when it is dislocated at other joints."
"Chapter XXX."
"Of the treatment of dislocation of the spinal vertebrae."
"When complete dislocation occurs at one of the vertebrae of the spine or neck, and several vertebrae are displaced from their proper places, then there is no treatment for it, because death usually ensues in such cases."
"And the sign of this is that the evacuation of the patient's bowels is involuntary and he cannot retain it. And many times some of his limbs or both sides of his body or both arms are paralyzed, or one of them; or one vertebra is displaced from its proper place, because many times displacement occurs in four directions."
"Therefore, this condition is already displaced towards the back and is called 'gelbozitat' [spasmodic torticollis]."
"But the treatment of it is that you should first consider whether the condition occurs in childhood. For if it does, there can be neither cure nor recovery from it."
"Yet in that which comes through sneezing or percussion or similar to those, the Ancients already spoke of various ways of cure with prolonged speech; yet this has been abbreviated from that which excuses little for its excess, due to my proximity to my intended exposition, with which I have explained and formed diverse instruments to show and explain this."
"And only that which happens properly on the back is cured by it, which I count."
"And that the illness be extended over its face, over the cheek near to the wall, and be extended far from the wet instrument so that the chest is not struck by it."
"And properly a straight staff should be placed, fixed in the earth at the end of the elbow around the head of him, and another staff should be placed in front of his feet at the other end of the elbow."
"And let the minister hold the staff, and not be fixed in a hole; and another minister hold the other according to that form."
"And properly wrap around the head of the sick person and remove from his temples the linen bandages and tie it; and extend the end of the bandage to the staff which is above the head of him, and tie that to it; and properly stretch the other bandage over his buttocks on both sides of him, and above his calves; and properly adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other staff which is above his feet."
"And properly each minister extend the bandage at the beginning of the staff, and let not the two staffs and the bandages be removed from their places where they are fixed, but nevertheless not tied as we have said."
"And the physician place his palms on the spine with strength between that it returns, or that he places upon that one table, and properly adjust his feet upon the table between that it returns."
"But if it does not return by this method, then take a table whose width or length is about three brassas, and dig in the wall which we have said is near to the sick person a place in which the end of the table can be inserted." "And properly place the middle of the table on the spine, and the physician place his feet on the other end, and stretch well between that the spine is compressed and returns to its place."
"And if you wish, turn the twist which is revolved with the hand; and if you lie on the ground near the head of the sick person behind the elbow of two staffs, the length of which is one bras, and behind the head of him in the same way two staffs; and let the distance between each staff be a palm, and already it is made in each staff drawn in which turn turns, and each be well fixed on the ground so that it is not moved."
"And let there also be one round staff upon which the twist is made, and let it be revolved in the two holes of the two staffs."
"And at the end of it there is a hole in which the staff is formed, the length of which is a palm with that which is revolved; and in the two other staffs there is the same as that."
"And properly stretch the bandages which you have stretched on the feet of the sick person in the hole which is above his head, and the bandages which you have stretched on his sides in the turn which is above his feet."
"And properly over each turn let there be one minister who turns the turn with his hand, and the physician compresses the spine as we have said."
"And after the spine has returned, and the place is covered, then it is necessary to apply an empyastic plaster with egg white, and then cotton; and then place a table over the plaster whose width is about three fingers or environ these, and its length is as much as the place of the swelling, and on the parting of the spine so."
"And then let it be bound with linen which we have spoken about, and use the subtle nourishing medicine within until it is healed."
"And if there is some prominence in the place at the end of the healing process, then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and cleanse with administration of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already a lead plate has been administered in that place."
"And sometimes prominence occurs in the last of the spines of the back, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that bone which already protrudes does not need to be pushed with this medicine, for otherwise you will cause your own death."
"Chapter XXXI."
"Of the treatment of a dislocated hip."
"You should know that the junction of the hip and the junction of the muscle do not ordinarily endure except in dislocation, and it does not occur at those parts which occur at other joints with small movement and concavity."
"And the junction of the hip is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated either inwardly or outwardly, or anteriorly or posteriorly."
"More often it is dislocated inwardly, and less so anteriorly or posteriorly."
"And the sign of dislocation towards the inside is that when you press the healthy hip against the diseased one, it feels longer and its prominence increases more than on the healthy side, and the diseased leg cannot be bent at the knee, and this is the place where one usually expects an abscess to form after cautery, because the head of the matter has already come here."
"And the sign of that one who experiences dislocation outwardly is that his symptoms are opposite to those of this condition."
"And the sign of that one who experiences dislocation anteriorly is that he stretches his leg completely but cannot bend it without pain at the prominence, and if he tries to walk, he cannot move forward at all; and his urine is retained and his swelling is abscess-like, and he walks with a limp on his heel."
"And the sign of that one who experiences dislocation posteriorly is that he cannot extend his leg nor can he bend it anteriorly any more than he can bend his knee, and his leg feels shorter than the other one, and his swelling is softened, and the head of the matter is towards that place."
"However, the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you should consider at first whether the dislocation is old and ancient, which has already happened long ago before your arrival. Then you should perform reduction according to his condition, but not in such a way as to be complete; for there is no need for great effort in this kind of treatment."
"However, in that one whose dislocation is recent and belongs to the four types of dislocation, then you should have recourse to this: return the junction and stretch it towards the inside and towards the outside, bringing the leg back to the right and left sides, for strong pressure will suffice and there is no need for further treatment."
"But if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare strong leverage and stretch the leg as far outwards as possible, or use his hands to do so, or use a rope which is tied above the prominence of the thigh; and another assistant should extend one hand over the diseased side so that he places his fingers behind the buttocks. Then press strongly with a linen cloth on the head of the matter and have another assistant hold the end of the cloth."
"And let the extension be to the angle between the thigh and the shinbone, or from the inner side of the thigh to the angle of the shoulder-blade, or from behind to the hip."
"And let all these be done at once while the patient is elevated with his body lying on the ground and remains suspended."
"This type of extension applies to all four types."
"And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, that is good. But if not, then it is necessary for each type to perform its own specific treatment."
"In the reduction of this proper dislocation, when it occurs on the inner side, let the patient be propped up on his left side. Then wrap cloths around the head of the matter at that place which lies between the head and the area behind the knee. Then extend the cloth upwards from the angle of the thigh to the highest point of the shoulder-blade."
"And then let another assistant strongly grasp the patient's arms and gently move the large part of the diseased matter towards the outside with violent extension, for it will return to its place."
"This type is easier to treat than the other types in reducing this member."
"But if it is excruciating for you and does not obey this type of treatment, then both feet of the patient should be tied together with a strong, light linen cloth around the ankles and knees."
"And let each foot be tied with a rope about four spans in length, and let the diseased leg be stretched out more than the other by two fingers' breadth."
"And then let the patient be suspended head-downwards from the top of a pillar so that he is in the middle; and let his length from the ground be about two cubits."
"And then command a strong youth to press the head of the matter and another youth to suspend himself along with the patient. Then let the other youth pull the matter forcefully with his hands, for the junction will return to its place quickly."
"But in the proper reduction when it is a dislocation on the outside, let the patient lie on his back according to what we have recommended previously in cases of stiffness. Then press the cloth on the diseased leg properly and on its foot."
"And then two pillars should be placed, one at the foot of the leg and the other at its head. Then another pillar should be placed in the middle on which damp cloths are wrapped so that the patient is not harmed by the friction between his thighs."
"And then each assistant should extend to their respective sides, and the physician with his hand shall guide the reduction according to the art."
"And when the dislocation is on the front side of the thigh, then let it be extended gently. And this method applies particularly to cases on the hip. Then the physician places his right palm over the diseased knee and holds it with his other hand and with that grip extends it from the inside towards the buttock."
"And if the dislocation is on the back side of the thigh, then it should not be extended from the inside outwards. Rather, let it be elevated off the ground and placed on a hard surface, just as we have described for setting the ankle bone when the ankle joint is dislocated externally."
"There should be administered compression with a board over the location where the joint is."
"And once the reduction is completed as needed, you know that the sign of having successfully reduced it is when you extend the patient's legs and when they raise their leg, the limb should return to its place without any difficulty. Then you apply two compresses and bind them with a bandage so that the limb is not moved in any way; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days."
"Remove the bandages and compresses, then compare one leg to the other."
"Now, if you see those symptoms in their entirety, you will know that the dislocation is fixed, and then let the patient go."
"If you see any signs of inflammation, then return and compress that again, extending it according to your first traction method, and leave it for three days."
"And then remove the bandage, and have the patient walk around slowly for a few days until they are comfortable."
"Chapter .xxxii."
"On the treatment of dislocation of the jaw."
The dislocation is of three kinds: for it is displaced at the outer parts, and at the inner parts, and at the parts above more than at the parts behind; but it is not displaced at the front parts.
The sign of dislocation is that when you tell the patient to press their leg against the couch, they know that their hip is dislocated.
And the reduction of all kinds is done thus: Make the patient lie on their right side so that they can extend their leg if possible; bring your left foot forward and place it between theirs; press your left hand onto the front of their hip bone, grasping it firmly with both hands, then lift up sharply while another servant presses down on the patient's leg behind you to help return it to its proper position.
The sign of reduction is that their leg adheres to the couch without pain or difficulty when they lift it up.
Then plaster is applied and the leg is immobilized against the couch; then it should be bound up with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbound.
And the patient should not use their limb for walking for some days until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction of it is impossible for you, then apply strong extension with ligatures as in the previous narration on the treatment of hip dislocation.
Chapter XXXIII
Of the Treatment of Ankle Dislocation
The ankle sometimes gets dislocated by a slight sprain, and sometimes it becomes completely dislocated; the dislocation may occur either on the inner or outer side.
The sign of ankle dislocation is that the area around the displaced talus appears swollen and inflamed.
But the treatment for a sprained ankle is easy reduction; it should be extended gently with the hands and pressed inwards until it returns to its normal position.
But in the treatment of a completely dislocated ankle, it is necessary that you make the patient lie down on their back; then have another strong and brave servant hold firmly behind them at the middle of the ankle.
Then you should place your right hand under the upper part of their foot and your left hand on the outer side of the foot at the location of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and push it two times with your left hand, then extend it with your left hand; then press firmly downwards on the heel of the foot with your right hand without shaking.
And repeat this process twice as we have described.
Then press the heel of the foot down to the ankle for the third time and you will be at the heel.
Therefore if it returns again or twice after this manner, and if the foot swells, it is good; otherwise, then the operation is repeated, for that returns.
If it is forbidden for you to reduce the hernia with what we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their side on the ground, and stretch a rope firmly tied to the ground between their thighs, and you have wrapped around them a cloth so that the patient does not slip.
And then the minister of the testicles holds his own parts, and another minister extends the patient's leg with their hands or with some device placed on the knee; and each minister stretches out their part as much as they can, so that the body of the patient is not pulled towards the sides due to their extension.
And then the physician reduces the dislocation with his hands, and another minister holds the healthy side on the outside, for the dislocation returns easily.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and it is declared to you that the reduction has failed, then apply an emollient plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the leg with bandages to the bed.
And be careful or guard yourself from the nerves which are behind the heel, for the bandage over it should not be tight and harm them.
And then leave him like that for two days or three; and if the bandage becomes moistened, remove it.
And then untie him on the third day or the fourth, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if it is necessary for him to walk during that period, it is not certain that the dislocation will not dissolve and be corrupted, and therefore he would not receive any cure.
And if an abscess appears from the wound, then you should apply in the cavity of it what is mentioned above in another place regarding cure and dressing, until it is removed.
Chapter XXXIV
Of the treatment of dislocation of the said feet.
It is necessary that he who has been dislocated be reduced with little extension without struggle; and what is not difficult, let it be done gently.
But if the dislocation is in some of the toes of the foot, then you should have the patient place his foot on the ground or on a table and stand up like he is walking.
And then you stand on one foot, and place your foot over what you imagine is the prominence of the joints, and press it down with your foot with force until it returns, and see that it has been reduced and that there is no prominence appearing in the location.
And then place below the belly of the foot of him a table that takes all the foot, with two ends, and press it down with proper and firm strapping for three days; and then untie it and let him rest from walking for more days until it is strengthened and the reduction is ensured.
Chapter XXXV.
"Of the types of dislocation that occur with plague or fracture or in combination."
"When some cause of those occurs, and you attempt the cure of it and the restoration of that, many times death ensues."
"And therefore it is not necessary to presume about the cure of that, but only he who is skilled in the art, with long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not hasty nor presumptuous; and let him use remedies at the beginning that are limited to drawing out the apostumes alone, leaving the patient to divine judgment, rather than hoping for health from destruction, with alleviation of the illness, and where there is some hope." "And therefore strive immediately to reduce it at the beginning of the cause before an abscess forms."
"And then if the limb returns according to our desire, we should immediately administer treatment that draws out the abscess and heals the plague with what is appropriate for it of the drying medicines."
"And if dislocation occurs with fracture and the bones are separated, then you should attempt the reduction of them, and do in that what we have said concerning the simple diseases of those parts that preceded."
"And guard and avoid according to your ability that which enters through the paths of fear according to what has been preceded by my instruction; for this is better for you, as you owe it to your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and healthier for your true body in your time." "So endeth the words of Albucasis, from which God grant blessing." "Amen."
